sLoan Man%s  Us$ 20P million equivalent, to be repaid in 17 years, including
5 years of grace, at the ptandard variable interest rate.
R"l1Sds2g     For the Project s private sector credit program component, BR
Tom$s         would relend US$ '12 million of the proceeds of the loan in local
currency to participating financial intermediaries (PIle) and
charge at least'the fully variable average deposit rate of the
Colombian  banking ,ytem  (.TF)  plus  a  margin  to  cover
administrative costs and foreign exchange risk if denominated in
Peso or, if denominated in US$ equivalent, a rate reflecting at
least the Barkl s interest rate plus a fee to cover the cross-
currency ri4t and RR's administrative costs. Relending rates to
enterprises would be determined by the PF2. according to the
maturity, credit risk, operational cost and competition of each
investment loan up to a maximum of six percentage points above
SR's rate to the PFI. The ceiling on the spread has been set to
equal or exceed the current average free market spreads. For Peso
loans, SR would bear explicitly the full foreign exchange risk,
and would bear only the cross-currency risk for US$ equivalent
loans. For the public sector support component, US$ 8 million of
the loan proceeds would be made avallable by BR to the Government
on terms identical to BR's repayment obligations to the Bank.
2MrlA&Uos  The proposed Project Is intend4d to provide capital and other
support to assist the private sector in renovating existing
industrial production capacity so as to reduce costs, increasing
product quality and service, building now capacity for goods in
which Colombia has a dynamic comparative advantage, or phasing out
capacity which ie unlikely to produce acceptable long run'returns
in an Internationally competitive business environment. The
Project incorporates those public policy and regulatory
adjustments in the areas of external trade, domestic competitlon,
the finanoial sector, labor and technology needed to achieve its
objectives. Most of these adjustments have already been achieved.
The Project would also includes   (a) a private sector credit
program to finance investments an4 technical assistance at the
enterprlse level and upporting sezvices at the subsector level,
and (b) programs to facilitato labor force adjustment,
environmental pollution control and Project monitoring support.



hlggk Riuha* The main risks to successful Project implementation are that (a)
revrsals may occur in the Government's trade refom program; (b)
PIls are reluctant to lend for restructuring investments because
of higher credit risks; and (0) investments are inadequately
prepared. To mitigate these risks, the trade program has been
publicly announced, is well underway and the Project includes
safeguards which would tie the Bank's loan to the maintenance of
the trade program's progress., The Project enables Pile to chare
rates according to credit risk up to a maximum which would equal
or exceed current average free market spreads, which is nearly $
percentage points higher than the previous Bank credit line to
Colombian  industry.    Finally,  during  Project  preparation,
restructuring issues were identified under five subsector studies
and steps initiated in part as a demonstration to formulate sound
restructuring strategies.   An agile pre-investment financing
mechanism has also been included in the Project to finance pre-
investment work by enterprises.

iNDUSTRIaL ESTRUCI URIN ANDo -DNLPIEENT PROJUCT
1.    In Febru.ty 1990, the Government of President Virgillo Barco set in motion
a program to liberalize the external trade regime, accelerate the  "eform of
public services and develop an internationally competitive private sector. The
announcement marked recognition by many policy-makers and private entrepeneurs
that Colombia's socio-economic objectives for the 1990s---to accelerate the
country's rate of economic growth and employment creatior, lower prices to
consumers, and further diversify the nation's export etructure---cannot be met
by continued high trade protection, import substitution, and capital-driven
growth. The Government of President Cesar Gaviria, which took office in August
1990, has reaffirmed its support for this program and accelerated its
implementation.
2.   The proposed Bank loan for an Industrial Restructuring and Development
Project (XRDP) is part of a package of policy and institutional adjustments to
facilitate  the realization of this program.   Sector wQrk  t and policy
discussions with the Government have beon intensive over the past two years on
trade, industrial and financial sector issues. A Bank loan of US$80 million
equivalent was approved in 1989 for a Fifth Small and Medium Scale Enterprise
(SME) Project, and a loan of US$300 million has recently been approved to support
broad-based public sector regulatory and enterprise reforms. Wo other projects
are expected to be ready soon which aim to facilitate the reorganisation of
Colombia's industrial development bank for large scale enterprises, the Instituto
do Fomento Industrial (IZrv, and to promote export development.
3.   The IRDP has been designed in close collaboration with the private sector,
upon which Colombian Industry depends almost entirely, and represents a
significant departure from previous Bank support for industrial development in
Colombia. Building upon the past sequence of Goverament reforms in the financial
sector,  corporate taxation  (1986), foreign direct investment  (1987) and
receivership (1988), and in tandem with trade reform, it promotes domestic policy
and institutional changes which seem to be the most essential for industrial
restructuring and developmant to succeed. 2 It also gives particular attention
to instruments needed at the enterprise level to prepare and implmaent
restructuring investments.
4.   The first two chapters of this Report develop the justification for the
Project by reviewing past sector performance trends and the present competitive
position of selected industries, then outlining the public policy and
institutional support framework for sector-wide industrial restructuring.
" "Colombia: Industrial Competition and Performance" (Grey Cover),
June 29, 1990.
2 For background, see 0 Industrial Restructuring-Policy and Practice",
March 20, 1989, and "Strengthening Trade Policy Reform", November 1989.

1I  T mm NED FOR INDUSTRIAL RS!RUC!URING ANDM 
S.    At the sector-wide level, industrial restructuring is viewed as a long-term
effort to align the industrial apparatus with the country's comparative
advantage. The Colombian Government's decision to undertake this wide-ranging
process recognizes the weak competitive position of its industry as well as an
acute sense of a number of well-known international trendsJ the technoloqical
revolution, particularly with respect to microelectronics, information and
communication, and biotechnology; the prominent success of and benefits accruing
to many countries employing export-oriented ievelopment strategies (such as those
in East Asia); the opportunities which may arise with the consolidation of the
European Common Market scheduled for- 19921 and the increasing emphasis being
placed by nations (such as those of Eastern Europe* on marketplace competition.
Its decision also reflects its understandLng that the long-standing inward focus
of its economy and the present intensity of internai .onal industrial competition
are likely to require a complex and all-embracing restructuring offort.
6.   The growth pattern of overall industrial production in Colombia shows
substantial volatility over time. still, it. production structure has remained
relatively static since the mid-1970s and of modest importance to the economy,
contrasting sharply with the dramatic cha-nge which have taken place in many
newly  industrialized  countries  (NICas).     Production  facilities  have  a
preponderance of older equipment and output has been- concentrated increasingly
in a limited number of plants. Industrial output has been focused consistently
on the domestic market and has failed to generate 4    Ievel of exports which would
re4uce ColombiaJ s heavy dependence on commodity exports.   The competitive
position in international markets of a number of important Colombian industries
is, on average, quite weak. However, with concerted action to deal with a
variety of restructuring issues, it appears that industrLalists can gain and
maintain comparative advantage in many product lines in a relatively open
competitive environment.  These points are discussed below.
1.1   Volate 291=   grh
7.   The gaowth of the industrial sector in Colombia ov- the past 25 years has
fluctuated substantially, largely as a function of shifts in domestic aggregate
demand and the real foreign exchange rate.   rollowing a substantial real
devaluation and estabishment of the crawling peg exchange rate system in the mid-
1960., the 1967-74 period was characterized by significant growth and
diversification of Colombian industry, both as a result of import substitution
and export expansion. Manufacturing output grew by 8.68 per year in real terms
(Tables 1.11 and 1.2). Thereafter, a highly overvalued exchange rate and other
macroeconomic problems asociated with a boom in world coffee prices in the later
19709 led to a fall in the industrial growth rate to an average of 4.1% over the
1975-19 period. With the persistence of an unfavorable macroeconomic policy
framework and the  international recession at the outset of the  1980.,
manufacturing output fell, by an average of -0.5%-per year during 1980-83.



-4-
8.    in 1984, the Governmant undertook a macroeconomic adjustment program which
restored internal and external balance in the economy as well as growth
momentum.3 one of its distinguishing features was the impressive growth of the
industrial sector, whlch registered growth rates averaging 5.4% over 1986-87.
In 1988-89, sector growth slowed to 2.3% and 1.7b, respectively, as the pace of
aggregate demand began to slackon. Most of the growth in industrial output was
achieved through the use of existLng installed capacity, idled since the early
1980., rather than substantial new investment.
1.2   fSta9aat Pgtuj"Lon Strugture
9.   Along its volatile gtowth path, the share of manufacturing in GDP has
remained relatively constant. It comprLsed 18% of non-coffee CUP in 1967 and 19%
in 1987 (Table 1.3).  Th  tagnation of Ladustry's share of GDP in Colombia
contrasts with the experience of many NICs, such as Rorea, Indonesia, ThaLland,
the Philippines, Turkey, andf Mxico, in which manufacturing growth has becom an
increasingly important contrtbutor to the economy (Table 1.4).
10. The structure of industrial production has also demonstrated remarkable
continuity over the past 15 years, again unlike many othe MICa. In the two
decades followLng World War 21, the concentration in traditLonal consumer goods
was substantially dlluted with growth in industrLes such as steel, chemicals,
petroleum ref in7Lg, and pper. The share of nondurable consumer goods in sector
output fell from 85$ in 1945 to 53% ln the early 19706. Since then, however, the
pace of diversification hai virtually stagnated (Table 1.S).  International
comparisons show that the she" of nondurable consumer goods in Colombia remains
high (50% in 1985) oompared to the averages of other Latin American countries and
all NICs (44% and 32%, respectLvely -  e  Table 1.6), where they have fallen
progressLvely. Colombian captal goods accounted for only 7% of output, compared
to 15% in Latin Amerlc ant4 26.% Li NICs.
11. There has also been little change in the size and geographLcal distribution
of plants over time (Tables 1.7 and 1.8). as would be expected, there are a
large number of small plants -nd few large plants, wlth the majority having 10-49
employees. Approximately one-third of all plants are located in the Bogota area,
23% in the Medellin area, and 11% in the Cali area. The four departments along
the Atlantic coastal area acoount for no more than 9% of all plants, confirming
the accepted notion that an4fcturing activity is heavily concentrated in a few
interlor regions of the country.
12.  Change  is evident, however, ln the age distribution of industrLal
facilities. While the proportion of new plants has remaLned falrly constant,
there has bee" a reduction In the share of plants betwoen three and ten years of
age, and a growLng shareof, p0ats over 11 years (Table 1.9).  The production
structure also show a high and increasing concentration of output in a limited
number of plnts.  In the cotton spinALng stage of the textles Industry, for
example, three firms represent more than 70% of total Lnstalled capacity. At the-
plant level, the concentration in also high, with more than 4S% of spindles found
in plants with more than a total of 40,000 spindles, cmpwared with 10% in Mexico,
3S"e the Cologbia CounWry Bconomic Memorandum, August 2S, 1989.

St in Spain, 20% in Turxey, 15% in the UK. This is a common phenomenon in
Colombian industry, as shown by calculations at the tour-digit 280 level for
19S8 and 1984 of the four-plant concentratlon ratio (CR4, see Table 1.10), whlih
reflects the proportion of subsector produption accounted for by the top four
plants.4
1.3  Dmetiga Mret Orientation
13.  The heavy dependence of Colombia's export structure upon coffee was
tempered in the 1980s by the rapid development of minerals, such as petroleum,
coal, nickel and gold. Their growth rates were particularly impressive over
1985-88 (averaging 23% p.a., se  Tables 1.11 and 1.12), and reduced the
predominance of coffee from Sl% to 32% of total expwrts over 1980-88.  This
achievement has been a key factor underlying the Governmont' aability to maintaLn
relative macroeconomic stability and to its decision to undertake trade reform.
Still, by the end of the 19809, Colombia's comtodity export dependence remained
higher than in 1975.
14.  The growth of anufactured exports was high during the late 1960. and early
1970. 1l3-16% p.a. during 1965-74). Growth suffered over the following six
years, - particularly from the effects of overvaluation of the peso, as
industrialists lost market ohare to competitors in both export markets and their
own domestic market. rollowing the international recession and the Government's
1984 acroeconomic adjustment program, increm ints in industrial exports were
impressive (40% over 1986-87 and 17% over 1988-89,0.see Tables 1.13 and 1.14).
The leading industrial performers were the textiles, garments, leather, and shoes
industries (40% average annual growth in 1987 and 19e8), followed by lndustrial
chemicals (22%), and paper and printing (11%). Remaining industrial exports had
growth rates below 10% per year in nominal US Dollar terms.
15.  The most recent trends are a respons mainly to th. sharp real devaluation
under the adjustment program by a limited number of firms ra          than the
realisation of a broad-based commitment to the export market. Investigations
suggest strongly that exporting by Colombian industrieA has been consistently a
residual activity to balance supply with fluctuation ln domeatic market demand,
rarely contributing more than 10% to annual outpwi growth in any subsector.
Consequently, the export orientation of the industrial sector Iha remained low
(7.5% of domestic production in 197S and 6.3% in 1986, (ame Table 1.15). This is
ln contrast with many gICs, which have derived significant advantages for growth
and structural change from expansion in the international market. The lack of
commitment to export is partly due to the greater risk and initial information
costs involved in selling Ln external markets, but probably mostly expl6 ned by
the anti-export bia of the trade policy and infrastructure regime, whic ma ke
sales to the dcmesti# market signiiicantly  ore profitable than exforting.
1.4  Weak  aomuet4ie Position
16.  Industrial "competitionw in the global environment now demands not only
that firm minimise product cost, but that they also give equaL emphasis to
developing product designs carefully keyed to market nlchea and ever responsive
to changing demand, exacting high product quality, and achieving rapid

distribution and service times. To determine with some procision the oompetitive
position of a representative sample of Colembian industry in the global economy
and how to improve it, subsector studies were done during Project preparation for

activity across consumer, intermediate and capital goods, and enterprise size
(interviews were held with a total of 526 industrial firms, more than 30 public
agencies involved with trade, training, developmant banking, and with private
producers associations)I (c) a possible need for restructurLng, as Lidicated by
long-term growth, productivity and financial performanco trendsl and (d) a range
of likely restructuring strategies, from mainly expansionary ("offensive") to
primarily contractionary ("defensive") activities.
17.   Tht studies were designed to determine in detail (a) the degree of
competitiveness of local producers in the subsector in relation to international
levels, (b) the principal causes of gaps in competitiveness stemming from factors
within enterprises, market structure and public policies, (c) the potential for
improving performance and its socio-economic impact, and, in more general terms,
(d) policy changes and strategy options for restructuring and development.
18. The studies suggest that, while there is a range of enterprise performance
within each subsector, the average enterprise in most of these subeotors would
not at present meet one or more of the main criteria needed for competitiveness
in an open domestic market environment or in export markets. Average product
costs are in most cases above their NIC and other developing country comparators.
Factor productivity is generally low by international standards.5  Product
designs suffer basically from inadequate communication with external markets and
local institutional support.  Product quality is hampered both by inadequate
quality of raw materials and the aging technology frequently in. use. Market
response time is uniformly high, due largely to organizational problems of
producers, public administration and infrastructure which are not geared to nor
disciplined by overseas markets. The studies also conclude that many firms in
these subsectors do have the potential to become internationally competitive with
existing or newly-developed product lines and, indeed, to generate growth in
local value added, exports and employment.  They further confirm that the
restructuring process can reduce energy cosisumption and environmental
contamination, frequently on a profltable basis.  Key restructuring measures
required to achieve international competitiveness in these subsectors are
reflected on the previous page. The conclusions of each of theqe studies are
outlined below (fuller summaries of the studies are provided in Annex 1) and help
inter alia to put into context the policy and institutional adjustments discussed
in the following chapter. These colusions do not necessarlX anoly to other
subsectors nor is taraetina of subsectors a 2art of the Project.-
5 These results coincide with an analysis of economy-wide productivity
changes by Garcia-Garcia (1988), which demonstrated that macroeconomic growth
since 1950 has been very costly, depending primarily upon additional inputs of
labor and capital rather than productivity increases to gen.*ate growth.'

24.  Clothing products are currently cost compettive iA relatlon to industrill
countrLes and most NICe, thanks mainly to competitLve sourly Wages in an industry
mainly of smaller enterprises and labor-intensive manufacturing (see facing
page). Yet, the sector has important weaknesses at the enterprise level which
hinder its overall competitiveness: doestic raw matkiai prices (about one-half
of product cost) are about 20-30% above prices in countries with competitive
prices; raw material quallty is frequently of inappropriate width, unevenly dyed
and with excessive imperfections; and factor productivity L low. te industry
suffers particularly from low labor productlvity as a oonsequence of a lack of
product specialization, insufficient amployee training, and inadequacies Ln
workshop organization and materials flow.  In addition, as in the textile
industry, the age of equipment is relatively high.
25.  In the external business onvironment, supply times are relatively long (60-
120 days) thanks to Colambia's sluggish export infrastructure.  This is a
critical bottleneck for a consumer goods industry in which "quick response"
delivery is an imperative.  Increasingly, retailers are obliged to compete
through reduced inventory management and cost while achieving fewer stock-outs,
better tracking of seasonal market trends and offering fewer discounts for unsold
merchandise. Caribbean countries, which represent Colombia's main labor cost
competitors at present, have demonstrated more responsive supply times and have
substantially increased clothing exports in the 1980. (Table 1.17).
26.  Given the importance of delivery time and Colombia's proximity to a major
market, the USA, reasonable air freight tariffs, and labor costs below a number
of major East Asian clothing exporters, the industry's export potential is very
good. These advantages contrast with the position of major Bast Asian clothing
exporters, which have higher productivity levels, and better quality' raw
materials and software, but are disadvantaged by higher wages, longer delivery
times and import quota restrictions.

continued focus on the already cost competitive cotton fiber-based clothing

31. With a saturated domestic market and idle capacity, the industry has
manifeated considerable interest in the export market and is likely to pursue the
following restructuring strategys (a) improve domestic leather quality through
consolidation of smaller tanneries, a price and quality classification system for
leather hides, access to leather imports to alleviate likely supply constraints
in the short run, and better quality domestic hides ln the long run, (b) launch
programs to establish standarization, quality norms and industry-wide fabrication
of lasts to permit the production of components for shoes and greater economies
of scale, (c) reconfigure plant layout and operations to increase labor
productivity, (d) establish subsector training, technology and extension centers
to increase plant efficiency, improve chemical treatment in tanning, and improve
quality control, and (e) develop an export promotion program to build upon the
initial export success so far..
32.   In the fruits and vegetables processing industry, backward integration is
low, quality of raw materials is erratic, and nearly all products are oriented
toward the domestic market.   Adaptive research of different varieties of
vegetables and trials of post-harvest treatment are inadequate, as are efforts
to control the Mediterranean fruit fly for fresh fruit exports.  Technology
transfer through the Technology Research Institute (IIT) is weak and, as in other
industries, agroindustry also suffers from poor transportation facilities and
capacity constraints in air freight (see facing page).
33. This indusOry appears to have good market potential in a number of fresh
and processed tropical fruits and in the *winter vegeteble. market. To realise
it, producers must increse and sustain quality through better selection of seed
varieties, adaptive research, cultivation practices and technology, and post-
harvest treatment.   In the remaining segments of the agribusiness chain,
entrepeneurs must expand collection centers/packing housee, refriger;ted
containers, cold storage warehouses, air cargo space, and links with importers.
Food processing and packaging facilities need to be updated, a uniform quality
grading system established, and existing R&D institutes reorganized.

35. Productivity is low compared to efflcLent international producers, as a
result of the older processes used and the poor condition of the facilities of
the country's only integrated producer, Acerias Paz del Rio (APDR). Colombian
iron ore costs are low on a per ton basis. However, the quality is relativewly
inferior and, together with the small size of the integrated steolworks
facilities, the advantage is lost. Electricity costs are comparable to other
developing countries (Venezuela is an exception) and lower than industrialized
countries. Locally purchased scrap is nominally at the international cost level
(though probably higher if adjusted for quality differences) while imported scrap
cost is more than twice the comparable international cost due to tariff and
transportation costs.
36.  Enterprise restructuring is expected to consist mainly of a (a) reduction
of duties on imported scrap, refractories and spare partsl (b) reduction in the
vertical inteogralon of APDR, streamlining of its administration and sales
operations, downsizing of its labor force, and modernization of APDR's production
facilities, reducing APOR's overall product ecosts by an estimated l0%s (a) modest
upgrading of facilities of the country's four mini mills, and (d) possible joint
venture with Venesuelan enterprises for certain products.
Vehicle Assembly and Parts
37.  Automobile and light trucks are assembled in Colombia by local affiliates
of Renault, Mazda, and General Motors, primarily through import of CRD
(completely knocked down) sub-assemblies and other components representing 80s
of all vehicle materials. -When taking "ito account the absence of a number of
product features found on most internationally traded vehicles (safety and air
quality features, powertrain and fuel injection technolgy, costing about
US$l,300-l,800 per car), local automobile costs are about 5% above vehicle
imports from the USA (se  facing page).   This reflects the international
efficiency in the fabrication overseas of the componnts, relatively low domestic
wages and reasonable operating efficiency for a labor-based aseembly operation.
38. However, assemblers are faced with the prospects of local market stagnation
at best. Thl country's market sise remains small (about 60.,000 units) and lacks
the large middle class needed to justify economically even one plant (150,000-
250,000 cars/yr is generally considered a minimum economic plant size). Export
market competition is expected to intensify with supply/demand trends and the
drive to compete based upon quality. And, most importantly, gradual reductions
in trade protection are expected to eventually lead to an increases in market
share by those imports offering a variety of styles, quality and options.
39.  The vehicle assembly policy framework utilized since the 1960G  by the
government is very similar to thoss applied in IICs with a large domstic market
for vehicles (such as Brazil, Mexico, Korea, Taiwan and India), which is designed
inter alia to stimulate significant output growth and transfere of tecbnology.
ColombLa's assembly regime has encompased a system of controls aimed to Lncrease
the scale of domestlc production as far as possible toward minimum efficient
levls, mainly by restricting the number of vehicles models and setting annually
the number of units produced. The promotion of local auto parts production has
been mainly tbrough trade protection, price controls, and integration

40.   In the global auto parts industry, competition is intensifying rapidly with
the proliferation in the number of models, the use of electronics, new materials,
and reductions in product cycle times (from about 7 to 4-6 years). Underlying
this is the revolution in industrial organization and management with such things
as procurement of sub-assembly systems from a few suppliers rather than a wide
number of individual components, just-in-time delivery and inventory systems,
flexible production runs of different models without retooling, exacting quality
and rapid order-to-delivery cycles. Market entry is made even more difficult by
requirements that auto parts suppliers undertake a considerable share of the
product development mad technological innovation work. In practice, this is
being accomplished by only a few developing eountrles, such as Braxil, Mexieo,
S. Korea and Talwaa, whleh have strong foreign direct lnvestment commitments by
clobal vehlcle companies due in part to the saze of the domestlc market and their
vehlcle exports.
41. In this environment, the present production structure of ColombLa's auto
parts industry is, for the most part, poorly positioned for international
competitlon. Labor-intensive assembly of parts, such as wire harnesses and seat
assemblies, do have a cost advantage over imports.   'owaver, more capital
intensive products with local raw materials, such as metaI-finished parts (e.g.,
fuel tanks, shock absorbers) and plastic molded componenta (trim, door panel),
are not internationally competitive (see facing page). 2a)w. sector is limited in
the medium-term basically by Colombia's relatively modest mLnufacturlng capacity
(size, organization, and technological level), lavestment requlrements ln product
development and engn"eri7ng, and an inabillty to attract major producers in a
joint venture to produce components fot new automobiles.  They are further
disadvantaged like other industries in Colombia by non-competltive prices for
many locally produced intermediate inputs (Table 1.18).
42.  A restructuring strategy for thli  lndustry is expected to include
progressive decontrol of regulations on vehicle assembly.   ThLs would be
complemented by a private-sector driven program to encourag a shift ln the use
of lnstalled capacity toward the production locally of trucks and buses, which
appears to be a "natural" economic activity and in which major fleet renovation
is required. Labor-intensive assembly rather than economies of soale is a key
factor conditioning comparative advantage, whlch li apparent in the few heavy
duty trucks now assembled (12% Cost advantage over -mports) and lnitial
projections for a local bus productlon program (24% cost advantage).
43.  Restructuring of Colombia's auto parts industry should be founded upon the
phasLng out of integration requlrements for the inclusLon of local parts Ln local
vehicle assembly. It would strenuously ptomote a strategy fopuaing on a market
OnLche* which requires the production of low-technology, labor-intenslve
replacement components for automobiles, which has modeet demands' related to
cost, quality and dellvey. The Us market alone is large (about US$25 billion
/year) and has provided substantial export revenue for highly supcegsful
countrLes such as Taiwan (US$400 millon/year).  Pursuing-this strategy will
entail malnly (a) productlvity and operational iprovments to improve cost and
quality competitiveness, (b) consolidation and closing of non-competLtive

suppliers, and (c) a well organized export promotion program for relatively low
volume parts in collaboration with independent distrlbution channels.   Local
parts producers would be expected to also build market share under the truck and
bus program,
First Steam Towaxg Restructurina
44.  Response to Project preparation work has been positive and wLdespread.
Agroindustrialists have prograimd aeminars in export marketing and quality
control, obsorvation missions to examine technology and marketing isues ln
various agroindustrial enterprises in Europe, market and product development
studies for canned vegetables, and preinvestment work for cold chain and
collection center needs. in March, 1990, the Fifth Congress of Textile Producers
adopted a proposal by the National Association of ntdustries (ANDI) to establish
a research and development center for textlles and clothing. Tho center will
offer consulting and training services on raw material quality and product
design, production processes and technology adaptation, equipment and finished
products.
45.  The auto parts industry has adopted a strategy which includes a program to
increase product standardization among producers, training and promotion to meet
export network requiremnts. for the aftermarket.  The leather lndustry is
refining its export market research and developing a technology conter, as is the
region of Antioquia in cooperation with a variety of industries and regional
universities. The shoe producers association, CORNICAL, has taken the ltad with
the support of its membership to establish two private sector-oriented centers
with a number of regional branches for technology and training and an, intgrated
export promotion program, particularly in support of efforts to increase product
specialization and components. Producers plan to learn from similar centers
already operating in Spain, Brazil and Mexico. Existing training centers oq the
industry are expected to be reorganized to-better focus upon enterprise n4e to
Increase the skills of supervisors and operators. The export program would
concentrate  upon  increasing  participation  in  international trade  fairs,
promotional programs and broker relationships, traiing in export markets and
mechanism, and improved airport facilities to handle exports.

46.  "Restructuring" by industrial enterprises is obviously a new word for a
very old concept. It describes a response, small or large, to changing
competitive conditions in the business environment, or initiatives to take
advantage of new basines opportunities. Todaya restructuring places a strong
emphasis on bringing the product innovation process closer to the customer,
reducing production errors, and reducing order-to-delivery and resupply times.
The focies is a much on organLzation and proce"s, or "software", as on fixed
production assete. As a consequenc  large contralized production facilities are
giving way to smaller Iunits, les  organizational stratifioation and more
functional integration. This permlts a shift from large production runs with a
wide range of different products to more highly specialized lines, able to
produce interchangeably different models according to market swings.- It also
leads frequently to the pooling of resources through strategic alliances and



-21-
equity contributions fo' "externalities", such as technology, product
development, training and information, as well as to a  haring of mrket
channels. This Chapter desoribes the public polioy, institutional, and service
support framework which will be needed to promote such processe on a sector-wide
basis.

sector programs for each Government, the ctual policy-making process has rarely
given high priority to nor been well coordinated from the lndustrLal perspective.
Productive communication and working relations betwen the Government and the
producer assocLations have been quLte weak over the long run. On the one hand,
the associatlons have sensed the low policy-making priorLty accorded to industry
by the Government, a lack of respons to their complaints of inefficient public

the inadequate capacity of the MiLnstry to address thear overall pollcy

These institutLons are expected to become the responsibliLty of a

51. However, the trade reform and industrial restructuring and development
programs formulated over the past two years reflect the most explicit, concerted
effort to define and redirect industrial policy since the adoption of the import
substitution strategy in the 1950s. Inter-agency teamwork has been very active
and, in part through Subsector Working Groups composed of public and private
sector representatives, the dialogue with Industry has improved on policy issues
influencing competitiveness. The Government and producer associations seek to
iaintain consultations within the Subseotor Groups as restructuring and
development proceed under the proposed Project.
S2.  Still, implementation of these programs through daily administration and
coordination will require a considerable boost in the present capacity of the
Ministry of Economic Development. While the Ministry was recently reorganised
on paper to give more attention to technology, mall and medium scale industry,
international trade, and industrial free sones,   its human and. budgotary
resources have changed little. Strengthening It in the coming year is essential
and is addresed through a Project Monitoring Program under the Public Sector
Support Program Component of the proposed Project in order to better (a) monitor
industry's response to trade reform, (b) maintain regular consultations with
producer associations to identify fine tuning measures to reducf' bottlenecks to
the supply response, and (c) increase coordination with other Governmental
entities to encourage further changes in policies needed to achieve international
competitiveness.
S3.  As the previous Chapter and Qolombian entrepreneurs themselves emphadsie
clearly, the enterprise restructuring and development proces is ncessary but
far from sufficient to achieve international competitiveness. Basic comparative
advantages of Colombia, such as low unit labor costs, abundant raw materials
supplies and good geographical position, hive frequently been offset by factors
which are outside the control of enterpries. Public policy and institutional
factors which diminish compatitivenes express thmselves in many forms-

2.2            sca EiniZaik
55.  During a process of major restructuring, industrial enterprises are more
vulnerable than usual to. significant:change  in mcroeconomic trends.   A
substantial  egmuet of Colombian  industry can be expected to undertake
investments with higher risks as they increase their production orientation
toward export markets and face greater competition inL domestic markets.
Maintenance of a s- able and competitive fq,reign exchange rate, conistent with
changes in the trade; regime, and adequato publi¢ budget dLcipline are
particularly crucial to the success of Lndustrial koftruoturing. The ColombLan
overnment has demonstrated relatively sound acrcoecenoz4c *anagement over the
past flve years, partlcularly in the face of several shocks.  This setion
descriItos briefly recent macroeconocic performance an the Gcoverment'  mediumr-
term policy framework within which restructuring Ls expected to proceed.
56.  Following a period of serious economic crisis in the early 1980. and
sweeping macroeconomlc reforms in 1984. -Colombia regained macroeconomic stability
and reduced sharply fLscai deficits to an annual average of 1% of GDP. CDP
growth reached an annual average of 5.6% during 1986-87, the unemployment rate
was reduped by 5 percentage points to about.10%, inflation was kept at about 22%
annual average, the current account balance improved from a deficit of 6% of GDP
to about balance, and international reserves recovered to above 5 months of
imports of goods and services.
57.  Recently, however, macroeonomic stability hae beon seriously threatened
by food shortages and the laggLag effects of an expansbonary credit policy in the
second half of 1987, which spurred inflation of 28% in 1988. A decline in the
coffee crop and coffee export quotas and a reduction of petroleum output and
exports contributed to an increase ln the flscal deficit to 2.8% of GDP and to
a deceleration of the rate of real GDP growth to 3.7* in 1988.  The collapse of
the International Coffee Agreemnt and the surge in mid-1989 in the conflict
between drug traffickers and the Government compounded this weakened performance.
The economic authorities reacted quickly to reverse these trends with a
tightening of credit policy, investment cuts, and lower subsidies. By year-end
1989, the fiscal deficit was held to about 1.8% of GDP and inflation to about
26%. Real GDP growth, however, slowed down to about 3% for the year.
58.   in 1990, the Governuemt adopted further measures, which lnclude: (a) a
devaluation of 6-8% in the real exchange rate by end-1990, representing a
decision to devalue the real rate beyond the December 1986 benchmark used so fart
and (b) enhanced fiscal austerity, inter alia by eliminating major subsidLes in
Ibid, page 54.

the coffee sector and enforcing a decline in the real domestic cof fee price,
holding down public sector wage increases to below expected inflatLon for 1990,
raising the domestic prle of gasoline and postponing public sector lnvestments
in selected areas. These measures together are expected to Lepresent a reduction
ln the consolidated non-flnanclal public sector defLclt by about 1.Si of GDP from
what it would otherwise were (2.7% instead of 4.2%) due to the Ocof fee drug"
shock. They represent a consLatent response to the external shocks, have been
dlicussed wlth IMF staff, and have been approved by the Fund's Board as part of
1989's Artlele IV consultations.
59.  The Government also adopted in 1990 an Economic ModernLsatLon Program
contaLnLng a set of structural reform in the public, trade, and flnancLal
sectors, as well as macroeconomlc polLcies deslgned to increase productlvity and
ralse economic growth to about S annually, slow inflation to about 20% per year,
and reduce the incldence of poverty. The centerpLece of the Program is a five-
year trade reform program aimed at increasing the competltlveness of the
tradeable goods  sector.   Complementary  financial sector and  industrial
restructurLng polLcies are designed to improve resource moblILzation and ensure
an adequate supply response of the productive sectors. Public sector reforms,
to be supported by the proposed operation, would Liprove the efflciency of public
sector services and institutions. These structural reforms would be underpinned
by stabilization measures in fiscal and exchange rate policies to maintain
internal and external balance.
60.  The medium-term macroeconomlc pollcy framework for the Program is based on
the continued pursult of prudent macroeconomic policies and the malntenance of
an adequate level of external competLtiveness through the implementation of an
actlve exchange rate pollcy. In addition to the beneficial effects on growth and
prLces, the implementation of these polLcies over the medium term is expected to
produce a decline in the external current account defLcit and the maintenance of
an adequate level of lnternational reserves, thus contributlng to the achlevement
of a viable external payments situatlon. The macroeconomic framework envisages
a gradual reduction of the nonfinancial publie sector deficit to 2% of GDP or
less in 1991 and a further deeline to no less than 1 of GDP ln 1992. Public
sector revenues would improve on the basLi of strict control of current
expenditure,  improvement  in  tax  administration,  higher  revenue  from
internatlonal trade taxes stemming maLnly from the replacement of quantitative
import restrictions by tarlffe, and adjustments in selected public sector prices.
The reduction ln the fiscal defLeit will enable the nonflnancial public sector
to elimlnate its need for credit from the domestlc fLnancial system, so that
expansion of financlal system credit to the private sector will be in line wlth
the growth of nominal GDP during this period. More detail on key macroeconomic
indicators and the balance of payments is contalned in Tables 2.1-2.2.
2.3 External Trade Reaime
(a) Imoort ComretLtion
61.  The trade reform program announced early in 1990 by President Barco
(*Programa de Modernizacion de la Economia colombLanaw) and reaffirmed by
President GavlrLa recognLzes that the tradltlonal import substitution model of
economLc growth has generated dlitortions in relative prices and has isolated
domestic production from Lnternational competition. It acknowledges that the
results of this strategy have been to limit economic growth, access to modern



-26-
technology, productivity, export grofth, end employment, and to increase costs
to consumers. Consequently, the program is to shift withln a period of Lve
years the regime from quantitative restrictions (GMs) to reliance on the forein
exchange rate and a tariff structure with modest protection level. This process
is expected to provide the incentives for industry to seek international
competitiveness and the lRDP would be carried out parL pasu with its
implementation. The following section describes the essential feature of the
program.
62.  Recoat Trade Policy Rfowms. External sector policLes over the last twenty
years have been tied to the macroeconomic management of cycles in agricultural
exports, particularly coffee. Rather than relying on macroeconomic policies to
smooth out aggregate expenditures in response to external payment deficits or
coffee boom8, impo* O and foreign oxchange controls have been alternately
tightened or eased.  The use of trade policy as a stabilization device has
resulted in the extensive use of QRs, uncertainty about access to Lmported
production inputs, and a highly volatile volume of trade.
63.  The  1984-86 macroeconoic  adjustment program  iacluded measures  to
rationalize and liberalize the trade regime. The program broke nev ground in
stressing the role of macroeconomic policies instead of QRs for stabilixing the
economy and its major achievement was the establishment and maintonance of a
competitive exchange rate.9 The program also reduced significantly both the
levels and dispersion of tariff rates: the average tariff rate fell from 61% to
30%, and the standard deviation was cut, in half between 1985 and 1988. However,
tariff surcharges were introduced in 1985 at a uniform rate of 10% and increased
to 18% in 1987.
64.  While these reforms were important, they mainly reversed the protectionist
trend of the early 1980. without liberalizing the importation of goods competing
with locally manufactured goods and changing the incentives for industry to
maintain a strong  inwird-looking orientation.   The coverage of domestic
manufacturing by import restrictions fell from 100% in 1984 to 82% in February
1990 (Table 2.3).'° The largest percentages of freely importable item  were in
intermediate semi-processed inputs, raw materials, and capital qulpment. The
most tightly restricted groups were finished goods, -which virtually gave local
industry protection from all competing impottp, - and food and agricultural
products. While such an indicator is only an approximation of the protection
effects of the import regime, it appears that it has been high by international
standards. For example, the coverage of domestic manufacturing by quantitative
restrictions over 1986-88 was 18% in Argentina, 41% in Brazil, 23% in Mexico, and
48% in Venezuela (Table 2.4).   Bolivia and Chile have no import licensing
requirements.
" "Colombia: Commercial PolLcy Survey", February 23, 1989 (Greoe  Cover).

related to each four-digit ISIC category that were either prohibited from import
or subject to prior import licenses. The QR coverage ratio is a weighted average
of the subsector values, using production shares as weights.

66.  Currint Trade poucy Reform.  The program adopted in February 1990 and
accelerated and extended to the agricultural sector by the new Governnmnt, aims
under two phases to achieve greater  neutrality in incentives, both across
products and across markets (doestic versus export), and to subetantially reduce
protection. It will alIso reduce administrative discretion-, achieving a more
transparent and automatic system. The program is complemented by the medium-term
macroeconomic framework described in the previous section of this Chapter.
67. under Phase 1 of the Program, import licensess were eliminated over March-
November 1990 and tariffs and-the exchange rate became the main instrument of
protection. The Goverrnment began in march 1990 to implemer.t Phase 1 of the
reform program and, through September 1990, had transferred 1921 positions from
the prior license and prohibited categories to the free list.* This increased the
proportion of freely importable tariff positions from 39% to 76% (Table 2.7) and
reducod the coverage of domestic manufacturing by import restrictions from 82%
to 44% (Table 2.3). Most of the remaining 1239 itiem (24% of tariff lpositionts)
on the prior license list, excepting imports associated with health and' security
And Some agjricultural products, were also moved to the free-list in November.
68.  Phase 2, to be completed by the and of the current Government, in 1994, will
increase the degree of import competition by gradually reducing the level and
dispersion of tariff protection, and by simplifying and Making more transparent



-20-
the tariff regime. The new Government announced in November 1990 average tariff
targets (including the surcharge) for the end of the trade program of an import-
weighted average tariff of 15% and a maximum of 25% economy-wide to be reached
by 1994. It also resolved to reduce the number of tariff categories to only four
by 1993. For those agricultural producta with substantLal international prLce
fluctuations, flexible tariffs are contemplated as a means of price
stabilisation. Over March-November 1990, the Government took -teps toward these
targets.  Twenty three different level were reduoed to ten. About 77% of the
tariff positions now have tariff rates les than or equal to 30% (Table 2.8) and
99.9% are less than 50% (automobiles are, to date, excepted).   Tarlffa were
reduced such that most raw materials and capital goods now have rates of between
5% and 10%. Rates of 20%-30% tend to apply to raw materials, inputs and most
capital goods with local production. Levels of 40%-50% cover most imported final
goods which compete with local production. The weighted average tariff for
industry has thereby decreased from 26% to 18% (Table 2. 5).  The 18% tariff
surcharge has also been reduced already to 13%.
69.  The program also includes several other steps to support the Implementation
of the trade reform process. To control under-invoicing of imports and to
provide information for possible cases of dumping, a price information system
will be developed to be used by Customs and INCOMUX. Other mechanism of control
would include the registration of bidders in the import license auction.
Customs administration would also be streamlined and anti-dumping legislation
will be prepared.

80.  An important complement to the trade reform program is a regulatory
framework whlch promotes competition within the domestic market. A number of
important domestic pollcy changeu have been made since 1986 by the Government to
reduce barriers to market entry and exit.   Reforms of. tie tax code and
receivershlp process were undertaken in 1986 and 1988, respectively, and price
controls have been progressively liberalLsed. This sectlons examines these
policy areas and their probable effects on the industrial restructurLag process.
(a) Zauthaa
81.  The maIn objectives of the 1986 tax reform (Law 75) were to achieve equity,
neutralLty cross forms of busines organliation and different economic sectors,
and admainitrative effLciency. in additlon, the tax reform sought to achleve
neutrality in incentivea with respect to a firm's choice of financing. Law 75
unifted the corporate income tax at 30%, reforming the prevLpus system in which
tax rates differed according to the form of business organization. It also
eliminated the majority of special exemptions and deductions that differed across
sectors. Regional and municipal taxes or infuttry and comerce are in general
relatively low and sectoral dlfferences are relatively unimportant. Sales taxes
are set at 10%, except for automobiles (20%) and some luxury goods (35%).

82. The principal cases of non-neutrality within industry are in the publishing

(b) ROc lvershLi
83.  The receivership process (Cgncordato) was established in 1971 as a
framework within Colombia I s CommercLal Code to glve pr9tection from creditors to
those enterprises facing or anticipating difficulties in the repayment of curreot
obligations, and the opportunity to r"gain long-term viability prior to filing
for bankruptcy. When an enterprise is admitted to receivership, debt collectlon
cannot be initlated and the enterpriese cannot be sued for bankruptcy until the
receivership process is terminated.
64.  The central problem with the system has been that the suspension -of a
firm's payments to its ereditors could in practice last anywhere from 6 months
to at least 6 year. In effect, it both acted as a barrier to market exit of the
enterprise and placed inordinate pressure on creditore to accept financial
conceseLons, such as lower interest charges and/or extended loan maturLties,
rather than promoting operational changes needed by the enterprise to compete.
These long-standing rules were changed by Congress with effect in May 1989.
85.  The incidence of fitms involved ln receivership has been appreciable. in
March 1987, 69 industrial lrfims had filed requests for protection i4nder

industrial corporations, partnerships and proprietorship re4gstered lith the
Superintendency of Enterprises. More than three-fcurths wer,. fond admon firms
producing textiles and4 clothiLng, paper, basic chemicals, cerAent and transport
equipment. By early 1989, the number of industrial firms had risen toA 105.

90.  The new law makes the Superintendency of Enterprises the competent body to
oversee mandatory reesivership.  In particular, the Superintendency is now
endowed with an administrative authority to docide on matters that previously
required the intervention of the district judge. The law also introduces a much
more rigid timetable within which the receivership process is implemented and
most of these decisions on the timing of the procedure are explicitly
characterized as not appealable. Violations of the timetable generally permit
the Superintendency of Enterpries to intervene to resolve the problem, including-
via the nomination of a co-administrator of the firm and plaoing an embargo on
fixed assets.  The law also requires the debtor to present a proposal of
agreement with its creditors at the time of application for admission into a
receivership process, which may be expected tobelp shorten the time spent under
receivership as it encourages the actual bargaining to begin from the outset.
91.  The modifications under the new law are likely to substantially reduce the
possibilIty of using receivership as a barrier to marIXet exit. Though its long-
term application will determine whether this is the case, an acceleration in the
processing of receivership oases Is already evident. Between May and December
1989, 23 cases reached the execution stage, of which 11 through liquidation, and
the number of cases still in process was reduced to 60.  In addition, the
Superintendency is facilitating the restructuring process through ths preparation
of subsector studies on competitiveness and, for those enterprises entering the
receivership process; by counseling enterprises on the range of services

available to help prepare financial and operatlonal restructuring plans under the
receivership process.
(c) Price Controls
92.   Price controls in Colombia have been in place since the 1940e on selected
industrial and agricultural products and some services. Until 1976, the National
Production and Prices Superintendency administered price controls. Thereafter,
this responsibility was assigned to a number of different ministries and other
Government institutions. The number of industrial producte that are subject to
price controls has been reduced substantially. Products previously controlled
that have been liberalized since 1976 include detergents and soaps, light bulbs,
taxis and other automobiles, tin and glass packaging materials, and garments.
93.   Currently, the main industrial products that are subject to price controls
administered by the Ministry of Economic Development are commercial transport
vehicles (buses, trucks, and campers), tires produced for commercial transport,
inputs for the tire industry, cement and cement products- beer and soft drinks,
and industrial ammonia.  Those administered by the Minlitry of Agriculture
include fertilizers and pOsticides; those by the Ministry of Education include
textbooks and notebooks; and those by the Ministry of Health include drugs and
pharmaceutical products.  While many Q4  these items are of some direct or
indirect significance to the,basket of consumer goods in lower-income households,
no particular and consistent rationale for price controls is apparent.
94.  Currently, the Ministry of sconomic Development applies an automatic
adjustment system under which the firms use agreed upon adjustment. formulas and
notify the Ministry of their actions. The formulas allow for annual adjustments
in the cost structure coefficients and attempt to acoount. for productivity gains
by limiting to 95% the proportion of input price increases that may be passed
along to the consumer.  Price adjustments are allowed no more frequently than
every six months. While this methodology represents an advancement compared to
previous systems, it captures only supply-side factors which would determine
market prices and ignore changes in demand or in the structure of markets.
95.   While the effects of price controls on investment and growth of the
industry are difficult to quantify, it is clear that enterprisq. in many of the
industries subject to price controls havy  maintained healthy profits.   The
profitability of the lPO largest firms in Colombia over 1985-87 suggests that
those that produce goods subject to price controls have had very good or at least
satisfactory levels of profits, implying inter alla that the price control system
as recently operated is not likely to have a significant negative impact on the
financial sector.  In addition, at the four-digit I8IC industrial subsector
level, the growth rates of production for goods subject to price controls,(with
the exception of rubber tires and non-alcoholic beyerages) have been above that
of the manufacturing sector as a whole.
96.  Under the Government' a, trade reform program, as domestically produced goods
are subject increaingly to -import competition, price controls to counter the
monopoly price effects of protection from quantitative restrictions will becom
redundant and are expected to be gradually phased out. Under the proposed



-36- -
Project, quantitative restrictions and price controls on domestic cotton
production would be replaced by a price stabilization scheme closely linked to
international price levels, and those on vehicles assembled locally are also
expected to be liboralized as part of adjustments in the sub-sector regime.
2.5  Financial Segtor
97.  The  success  of  industrial restructuring  and  trade  reform  depend
substantially on the full and broad-based support of the financial system. This
will require that financial lntermediaries have adequate incentivea and the
skills to finance restructuring investments which, almost by definition, are
likely to contain on average a higher level of risk than those encountered in the
past. Financial margins must reflect these risk levels and be complemented by
a minimum of regulation in credit lines designed to finance restructuring and in
their wanagement by the second-tier inatitution.   Intermediaries must also be
prepared to restructure existing indebtedness and require increased equity
investments in such enterprises to ensure an appropriate capital structure for
restructuring. This sectlon reviews the structure and past performance of the
financlal intermediaries concerned with the proposed Project, as well as key
policy issues and institutional development needs to ensure the appropriate
involvement of the system.
(a) Structure and Performance
98.  About 80% of the total assets of Colombia's banking system are held by
commercial banks, development banks, and the central bank (Sanco de la Republica,
or BR), which plays a signiflcant role in credit markets in addition to its
functlon as the monotary authority. Aavings and loan associations, trade finance
companies, insurance companies, investment and mutual funds constitute the
remainder. The 26 commercial banks domlnate the iihancial sector, accounted in
1987 for 60% of total deposits and 39% of total assets of the institutionalioed
financial sector (Table 2.10).   Four of these commercial banks have been
traditionally-owned by the public sector, and 5 more were nationalized after
1982, and consigning the private sector institutions to a minority role in the
sector.  Together, these 8 public banks accounted for 57% of the assets of
commercial banks at the end of 1989, in nearly equal proportions between,these
two groups. Of the remaining 17 private commercial banks, 9 (30% of commercial
bank assets) are Gwned br Colombian stockholders, and 8 (7% of commercial bank
assets) have foreign equity participation.

(b) Rg2ix Issuma
105. The Government has pursued a gradual program of policy reform in the 1980s
to reduce distortions and encourage greater reliance on market forces in the
financial sector. The burden of forced investments and reserve requirements has
been reduced moderately, there is greater reliance on open market operations as
an instrument of monetary control, interest subsidies on directed. credit have
declined, a deposit insurance qcheme has been created, and an initial growth in
capital markets is apparent. Attention has alpo be6n given to strengthening weak
financial institutions and to improving prudential regulations, with the positive
results for solvency and liquidity of the system decribed  ove.
106. All financial iwstitutions arn subject to the regulatory framwibrk of the
superintendency of Banks in Colombia. The Superintendency is responsible inter
alia for monitoring the procedures, practices#, solvency and liquidity of all
institutions in the financial system. It exercises this responsibility mainly
via (a) it. authority to,Approve the creation, restructuring and liquidatiop of
such lnstitutions, (b) setting regulatLons and techhical norms to meet standards
of operation and accountability, and (c) itb authority to suspend operations of
an institution found to be in violation of its statutes, flnancial or operational
requirements.' t&cilities are available through the Central Bank to maintain
liquidity requirements and, where in institiiion's financial and operating
condition  s endangered, the Superintendency 'has wide latitude to intervene to
protect the interest of cre4*tors 4nd maintain its viability.

worth requirements (defined as at least, 50 percent of paid-in capital), (d)

108. In 1989, a number of important regulatory reforms were taken. Among them
were (a) a further strengthening of capitalization by imposing a stricter
leverage ratio on commercial banks (from 15X1 to 12.5X1) and a modificatLon of
indicators of capital adequacy to reflect astets weighted by risk; (b) approval
by Congress of a law permitting foreign investment in the financial sector and
the re-privatizatLon of the five banks that were nationalized over 1982-85 (two
such banks, Tequendama and Trabajadores, are expected to be privatized within the
coming six months); (c) establishment of uniform accounting system for financlal
entities; (d) definition of stricter criteria for the evaluation of portfolio
quality and requirements for increased provisions; and (e) increased interest
rates and margins, introduction of an interest capitalization scheme, and reduced
regulation of industrial directed credit managed by OR.
109. However, major structural weaknesses remain in the financial system, which
result in a shortage of long-term credit, high credit cost and inadequate market
coverage. Consequently, the Government has prepared and agreed upon with the
Bank a Financial Policy Framework which sets out a program of structural reforms
in the financial sector intended to lead to a sector characterized by less
concentrated ownership, greater competitlon, a size capable of mobilizing
domestic and external resources to finance investments in the productive sectors
and a wide range of financial instruments provided at market prices by prlvate
financial institutions, inclUding voluntary long term ctedit and capital market
instruments. Under the Framework, the Government will expand the scope for the
use of market determined interest rates within the financial systemf, phase out
the policies of directed credit and forced investments that segment financial
markets, and improve the regulatory framework in order to promote competition.
110. To promote competition and contestability, the government will allow free
entry of new financial institutions; simplify procedures governing liquidations,
mergers, and conversions; reduce and clarify thie role of the public sector in
financial intermediation; encourage greater availability of market information;
and increase monitoring and cQ*trol of noncompatitive behavior. Competition
policies will include the re-privatization of five banks that fell under
Government control as a result of the 1982-85 financial crisis.   Reserve
requirements will be reduced as far as the fiscal deficit and monetary and
external conditions permit. Forced investments will be gradually eliminated by
freezing the nominal stock at existing levels, and in the future resources to
finance directed credit will be mobilized from domestic and international capital
markets. The reform of the directed credit system will be accelerated by first
gradually increasing rediscount rates to at least DTF and increasing interest
rates to final borrowers toward market levels and, by early 1994, fully,
liberalizing final rates so as to eliminate subsidies, except in the case of
certain well-defined target groups (presently confined to low income housing and
small agricultural producers) and harmonizing terms across sectors. In remaining
cases where the most efficient way to achieve equity objectives is through
subsidized credit, these subsidies will be carefully targeted and funded from the
fiscal budget.

116. Under the proposed Project, the remaining subsidies on BR directed

11 7. Forced  lavestemts.    In  addition  to  satisfying  monetary  reserve
requirements, Colombian financial intermediaries are required to place a portion
of their liabilities in low-yielding assets as forced investment and as
substitutes for reserve requirement (I18s). The rationale for this is to induce
the availability of long-term funds in a market which does not otherwise offer
them and to provide funds on preferential term to promote "priority' activities.
Commercial banks are subject to two types of forced investment:  (a) those
established by Congress to finance agricultural activities, the public debt, and
low income housing (Inatituto de Credito Territorial, or ICT); and (b) those
established by the Junta Monetaria to finance industrial activities, housing (ICT
and BCH), and to recapitalize the financial sector. CFs are forced to invest 8%
of the funds they mobilize with CDTs in category (b) and about 1.5% in 151. In
addition, they are forced to place 80% of their equity capital in equity in the
real sector. Insurance companies are forced to invest most of their technical
reserves in low yielding bonds of the Government and ICT.
118. The burden of mandatory investments and reserves has remained relatively
stable during the last four years at 28-30% of deposits for the financial system
as a whole. The average yields on mandatory investments has increased since the
early 1980., though they remain negative in real terms and are substantially
below their opportunity cost. As of September 1989, forcod investments amounted
to 6.4% of the total assets of the financial system and 2.9% of GDP. In the case
of commercial banks, they accounted for 14.9% of credit portfolio and offered an
annualized return of 16.5%, 15 percentage points -below the implicit average



-42-
return on their overall portfolio. According to recent calculations, '1 Lf
commercial banks had been able to channel their forced investment resources to
a loan portfolio equivalent to their average overall return, profits would have
increased 76%. Alternatively, Lf such proflts were utillzed nstead to lower
interest rates, they would have been reduced by 2.2 percentage points, from a
margin at that time of 14.6% per annum to 12.4%
119. The progressive lncrease in real interest rates Ln ColombLa, the inciplent
trends toward term transformation by the market itself, and the need for agLle
financing mechanisms by the private sector embarkLig upon industrial
restructuring make it imperative that intermediaries themselves begin under well-
defined conditions to lend long-term resources instead of investing in forced
investment instruments. This would both increase the financial incentives to
lend and substantially eliminate the restrictions and classifications now
exlsting under directed credit regulations on such funds. Flrst steps have
recently been taken by the Junta Monetaria to reduce by more than 25% the volume
of forced lnvestments In the financial system.  Under the Financial Policy
Framework, the Government intends to phase out by June 30, 1991 most remaining
forced investments (some agricultural credit will be excepted) by freeaing the
existing stock of such investments at their end-1990 level. At the same time,
it will act to increase the average return of those forced investments.
(C) Ingtitutional Development
120. 8ecurities Market. The Bogota stock market was created in 1929 and, by the
mid-1960s, stocks were the principal source of  financing  for corporate
enterprises in Colombia, with ownership dLtributed among 405,000 shareholders.
The size of Colombia's exchange ranked behind the United States and Canada ln the
Western Memisphere. Since 1970, the securities market has declined drastically,
coinclding largely with the creation of the directed credit lines of BR. In
1988, the value of shares traded was only about 3% of its relatlve size in 1945,
and listed only 86 companLes (Table 2.24). It had the highest concentration of
shares of all developing countries listed in the International Finance
CorporatLon's (IFC) Emerging Markets Data Base: about 22 stocks constituted
nearly 90% of market capitalization. Of the lnstruments traded on the Bogota
Exchange in 1988, 67% were short-term fixed income securlties, followed by Us
dollar acceptances (18%), long-term bonds and certificates (12.6%), and only 2.4%
in shares.
121. Until the 1986 tax reform, tax policy biased relative costs in favor of
debt rather than equity fLnancing by firms, and biased relative returns against
equity investments by individuals. Combined with the availability of credit at
subsidized rates from the directed credit system, it was rational for firms to
choose and, indeed, resulted in highly-leveraged capital structures (Figure 2.1).
The tax reform bill of 1986 was designed inter alia to alter the capital
structure of enterprises, by inducing new sources of equity for productive
investment and reducing the high leverage which made them and the banking system
vulnerable to non-payment of debt obligations. A consolidated view of 164 major
industrial enterprises shows that equity has increased in real terms by about 27%
in 1988 over the previous year and another 9% through June 1989 (Table 2.25),
" "Una Propuesta para Bliminar las Inversiones Forzosas", Hommes and
Montenegro, Banca Y Finanzas, December 1989.

(a) Labor Costs. ProduotivLty and Moblltv
130. The performance of the industrial labor force is a fundamental determLnant
of internatlonal competitiveness.   This section hlghlights the impact on
competitiveness and the restructurlng process of levels of remuneration, labor
productLvlty and the labor regime ln relatlon to labor moblity.
131. Remuneration. An analydsi of the relatlve trends over 1970-86 in real
total remuneration rates for Colombla's lndustrial sector suggest that the
average aggregate sector remuneration rate has Lncreased by a total of 14-38%
(depending upon the deflator used, see Table 2.26). Total remuneration hs not
grown as fast as non-wage benefits (NWB) and has not grown faster on average than
labor productivlty. The wage component of total remuneratlon showed a dealine
in real terms of 13% over thls period. In comparison with total remuneration
trends among 21 developing countries, such rates seem to follow the direction of
those ln several East Asian producers but to dLverge with other countries ln
Latin America, South AsLa and Africa, whore declines ln real labor costs are
predominant.
132. ComparLsons of absolute remuneration levels have also been made by product
group ln the five subsector studies (Table 2.27). They examined a broad range
of Lndustrial remuneration rates around the average rates ln Colombia, from those
for very labor-intensive actlvlties (clothLng and shoes) to those for highly
capital-intensive ones (steel and vehicle assembly). Their fLndngs suggest, as
expected, that average total remuneration rates are substantially lower than
those in industrial country enterprises engaged ln sLmLlar actLvLties. This i8
true even among capital-intensLve activities where, for example, unlt
remunration rates in the Us automobile ndustry and the steel industries of
Japan and Germany are about 450% of Colembian remuneration rates.
133. Far greater rlvalry exists between Colombian remuneration rates and those
in NICs and other economically developing countries. In the clothing industry,
average total remuneration in East Asian and the Caribbean countries (excepiLng
the Dominlian Republic and Haiti) tends to exceed the rates in Colombia (114-274%
of Colombian rates). In the other subsectors studied (where a more limited
number of comparators are available), Brazil tends to be noticeably more rate
competitive (30-92% of Colombian rates), Nexico wlthin a similar range (80-114%)
and East Asian countrLes at or markedly above (95-461%), depending on the product
line. Thus, the broad parity or advantage of Colombian total. remuneration rates
in relation to most NICe and other developLng country comparators tends to
provide a sense of comfort about the current impact of such rates on the
international competitLvenes of its lndustries.



-46-
134. Of course, this to a statio analysisd whloh does not antiCipate utur
trends. In some cases, as in the Case of the shoe ldustry discussed earlier ln
thls Report, lt is clear that exporters having experlenced relative lncreases ln
remuneratlon are shiftlng productlon of some product linoo to lower-wage

135. Non-Wage  Def its (WR).  Despite the modest growth slaco the 1970s in
total remuneratlon, the growlng share of MWM l a matter of slgnlflcant concern.
The Colombian labor force receives a wlde range of statutory NWs. Firms with
total assets of more than Col$200,000 are requlred by law to pay the following
allowances:
(a) Annual leave, wage primla (a Othlrteenth month"), famlly allowances,
soclal securlty (whlch includes sickneso and maternity benefLts),
oontributions to the Natlonal Vocational fraining Service (OEMA) and
Colombian Family Welfare Institute (IOU) and occupational dieease
and work accident allowances and old a%*, dlsabllity, and death
benefits; IJ 
(b) Severance payments, equal to one month's salary per year of service
to be payable when employment is terminated (including for reasons
of retirem_nt or voluntary reignation). Firms are requlred to set
up a reserve fund to cover future payments.  For entitlements
arising after 1962 (the majority of firms' obligations), such
payments are retroactives the base for the calculated payment is
the salary of the last month befqre termination (provided it had
remained constant for the last three months).   Interest (12%
annually) must be paid on the workers  balance in the fund.  In
additlon,  employees  may  make  partial  tlthdrawala  of  thelr
accumulated balancce for certain purposes (e.g., houalng); and
(c)  Compensation for unjustifled dismlisal:  these benefits include (a)
an indemnification ranglig from 45 to 615 days of salary, depending
upon the years of servlce, for dismissal without just causea and (b)
the right to be reinstated or, alternatively, to claim an extra
pension payment for employees wtth more than ten years of service
who have been dismlssed unjustly.
in addition to statutory NWBs, many firms (particularly larger firms in which the
labor force i8 unionized) pay  WBs in addit4ton to those required by labor
legislation and may cover everything from eyeglasses to school materials.
136. NWB have increased markedly in both real terms and as a proportion of total

Colombla's position has jumped from the lowest among six Latin American countries
in 1965 to the highest in 1985, exceeding asme of its key NIC competitors like
Mexico and Brazil  (Table 2.30).   Colombia's NMB/wage ratios were also
substantially higher than the East Asian countries of Hong Kong, Korea and
Singapore. MS ln Colombia have (a) driven increases ln real total remuneration,
(b) put pressure on the already weak capital structure of many enterprises, and
(c) lt appears, promoted large pools of teiporary labor instead of more stable,
though flexible working relatLonships with enterprises.  In the longer run
