138. Labor ProductLvity. 2..e subsector studies suggest that a fundamental and
immedLate obstacle to internatLonal competitlveness of the more labor-lntensive
lndustries ln Colombia is low labor productlvity (see facing page). In relatlon
to high-wage industrial countries with hlgh labor productLvlty, relatively low
remuneration levels in Colombia can often compensate for low productLvity and
yLeld cost-compatitive products. This is the case even in relation to the Bast
Asian NICe in the clothing industry, where Colombian wages are slgnifliantly
lower than their counterparts (Table 2.31).   Sowever, ln product lines wLth
similar wage ranges across countries (eq., textlles, shoes, wooden furnlture),
many countries of last Asia, Latin America and the Caribbean have achieved a
competitlve edge wlth labor manufacturing times per unlt of output which are
markedly lower than in Colombla.
139. Like remuneration rates, labor productLvlty trends are also very dynamic
(Se  previous Chapter).   Nhlle industrialized countries are lntroducLng
mlcroelectronice-based systems to reduce costs and increase responses to demand
changes, a new generation of developing countries, like China, IndLa nd
IndonesLa, is challenging the NICe. Therefore, even where lt may presently have
a productlvlty-adjusted labor rate advantage over some countries, Colombla must
continue to induce at least comparable productivity growth rates in its
industrLes over the 1onger-term to malntaLn and improve lts competitive posLtLon.
140. Labor Nobility. The influence of labor regulations on the competitiveness
of industrial enterprises goes considerably beyond remuneration and labor
productivity. It also has a distlnct impact, through the extent of the mobillty
of labor, on such issues as an enterprLse's (a) product and market strategy, (b)
economic Lsiz and organizational structure, (C) market response tlme, and (d) the
cost of capital stook (capltal productivlty (technology, maintenance and
equLpment operation)  ' utilization + duties  and  interest cout).   The
compatlbility of labor regulations with restructurLng requirements is essential
as, in the longer run, the combination should reduce employment losses which
would otherwLse result from the loss ln competitiveness in the marketplace.
141. The experience of a number of countries undertaking broad-based industrial
restructuring demonstrates that regulations need to be relatively flexible to
accommodate internatLonal competLtiveness consLderations.   In Spaln, three
decrees were adopted over 1982-84 (21, 8 and 276) to provide a framework with
lncreased fleaxbllity in labor regulations and support for labor as restruaturing



-49- -
proceeded in industries such as textiles, steel products, shipbuilding and
household appliances. It included provisions for extended financial support
under the social security system, professional retraining, and tax incentives for
capital investments which created employment in Urgent Reindustrialization Zones
(SUR). some 500,000 projects were approved for these purposes through 1989.
Framework agreements between affected industrial subsectors and labor
organizations were also established under Spanish legislation to determine and
monitor arrangements for dismissal and redeployment of workers. They focused
particularly on provisions for early retirement, severance pay, reduced work
hours, retraining and relocation ln the context of defined enterprise
restructuring plans.
142. Colombian labor regulations have been highly stable since the
constitutional changes of 1936 and, excepting one reform of the legislation in
1965, have been modified primarily to widened the coverage or form of their
application. Current legislation contains the regulation of labor contracts for
individuals (governing capacity, consent, form, duration, revision, suspension,
and termination); guarantees of the right of association and collective action;
regulations and guarantees governing grievance and other proceduresl and special
legislation governing labor rights and regulations in the public sector.
143. Labor legislation is heavily oriented toward job stability through
penalties and Government controls. Under this regime, industrial entorprises
tend to adopt a variety of measures to harmonize changes in business operations
with the interests of their employees---not replacing retiring staff, negotiating
early retirement and resignations with indemnities, and using increasingly
temporary labor and microenterprises. However, these tools are not likely to be
adequate in view of the expected breadth and depth of industrial restructuring
adjustments needed. The subsector studies and other works, including that of the
Chenery Employment Mission of 1986, indicate clearly that the present labor
regim acts as an important barrier to labor mobility, and is likely to be a
significant constraint to both the industrial restructuring process and,
ultimately, the success of the trade reform program.
144. The most binding features of current regulationa for restructuring are
summarized below:
o    emplaoye dismissal cannot be made without "just cause" (Article 40S
of the Labor Code) and without the prior consent of a labor judge.
The definition of "just cause* encompasses personnel regulations and
criminal grounds, but does not include among its standards the need
to adopt new technologies and make other adjustments in enterprise
operations to become competitive over the long-term.  Also, no
dismissals are permitted durtng the petition by workers to remain,
a procedure which usually takes 4-6 monthsl
o    collectlve dismissals must be justified according to a minimum
standard of force majeure and this action requires the prior
authorization of the Ministry of Labor and Social Scurity. Again,
this standard does not provide for steps to increase competitiveness
as a valid justificationu
o    unit closiugs also must be justified upon the basis of force majeure
or accident, and receive the prior authorizatLon of the Ministry;



-50-
o     labor contract durations are considered to be unlimited, except for
the hiring of labor to meet temporary needs for the production,
sales and transportation of goods. This can substantially limit an
enterprises ability to down-size its labor force when adjustments
to develop or maintain long-term international competitiveness are
required.
O    the transfer of employees cannot be made without just cause, or
without the prior consent of a labor judge;
o under the unait enterprise concept (unidad de egmresa), differentials
in an enterprise's wage structure and labor working conditions are
not permitted, regardless of the fact that. each affiliate or
subcontractor may have a distinct economic structure.   This
regulation impedes investing in regions with lower labor costS,
subcontracting and reducing plant size. Along with the previous
limitation, it may also limit severely the ability of an enterprise
to decontralize operations, which is a common and vital
organizational step in many competing countries.  It is also be
contrary to the promotion of increased employment in Colombia via
subcontracting with small and medium scale enterprises; and
o    obligatory r3eitegration after 10 years of service offers special
protection to workers with more than 10 years of service, who are
protected from dismissal without "just cause". This discourages
enterprises from keeping employees more than 1l) years and provides
substantial disincentives for retraining and retention.
145. The Government is quite conscious of the impediments of current iabor
regulations to achievement of its trade reform and restructuring programs.
Therefore, it submttted to Congress in September 1990 proposals to revise the
Labor Code so as to permit more flexibility in labor deployment. The following
specific adjustments (among oithers) included in the Code were adopted by Congress
in December 1990s
(a)  introduction of competitivenes restructuring, and "oodernization"
requirements among the criterla for "just cause" in cases of
employee transfer and individual and collective dismissal, and as
a basis for setting the duration of labor contracts, permitting
reductions or closing of production capacity, and adopting new
technology;
(b)  establishment of a schedule by enterprise size which reduces the
number of enterprise for which a review is require by the Ministry
of Labor and Social Security prior to collective employee transfer
dismissal, and for reduction or closing of production capacity. The
remaining reviews are intended to verify compliance with the new
definition of "just cause";
(c) suspension of the use of the "unit enterprise" definition for 10
years  to  permit  eparate  labor  contract  negotiations  with
economically distinct units of the same enterpriseg



-51-
(d)  allowance of the flexible defLnition of work hours and contract
arrangements (eg., limlted duration, part-time employment) ln
accordance with their production requirementsu
(e) elimination of obligatory reintegratLon of dismissed employees wlth
more than 10 years of service and the tpension-saction"' and
(f) elimination of the partlal retroactivity clause of the ceantla
system.
Adoptlon by Congress of these changes as a part of its reform of the Labor Code
would be a conditlon of Board presentation of the Loan.

146. Increased labor moblity needs to be counterbalanced by labor adjustment
assistance and specific training programs. Full restructuring under favorable
policy and enterprLse support conditions in the flve subsectors studled ia
estimated roughly to result ln a net increase ln employment of some 46,000
persons, or about 1St of the 300,000 workers presently engaged in these
industries (Table 2.32). Thls lncrease is almost entirely attributable to the
clothiag industry. It is expected to be accompanled at the same time in many
other industrLes by signlficant employee movement wlthin enterprises and between
enterprises ln the same or like 'industrLes---such as within shoes and leather
enterprises, from textiles to clothing firms, and from auto assembly to bus and
truck production plants.
147. other enterprLses may experlence a net contraction in labor force---such
as in the tanning, steel and autoparts industrles. For these three industrLes,
it has been estLmated that a contraction of the order of eight thousand workers
might result. This down-siring would probably take place at a modest rate over
a flve-year restructuring period, given present expectations about the gradual
pace of the trade reform program, and would constLtute about 3% of the labor
force of the five subsectors. However, it would represent almost 30% of the
employment of the three subsectors affected and it would be most unlikely that
it could be managed via the normal attritLon process. Other industries not as
yet closely examined may be similarly affected.
148. The Government seeks to minimise the socio-economLc costs incurred during
the trade reform and lndustrial restructuring processes for all of those
associated with this transition, and to promote re-employment as rapldly as
possible in labor-absorblng growth areas of the economy. Experience elsewhere
has shown that permanent economic benefits can be gained from re._mployment
promotion when laid-off workers acquLre new skill which make them ellgible for
higher quallty jobs, and offer more stable employment and/or higher wage rates.
149. Expllict program. to facliltate redeployment have been successfully
undertaken ln diverse and dLfficult situations, such as the Swedish shLpbuilding
Lndustry, whlch reduced its employment by over 80 (some 27,000 employees), and
the Corby Works of the Britieh steel Corporation (12,000 employees laid off),
bringing local area unemployment to nearly S0% from dlrect and lndirect losses
of jobs.  Frequently, the most severely affected have proven to be older
employees, women, and blue collar workers with low ekill Levels in larger
enterprises, who have more dlfflculty ln flnding new opportunities.

150. The design of each program Ls dependent in large part on (a) the overall
economic growth condltions of the area affected, and (b) the extent of employment
dependence on the enterprise(s) which are restructuring in relation to the ize
of the area's labor force.  In some cases,  uch as the Instituto per la
Ricostrusione Industriale (IRI) ln Italy, expliclt regional strategies are
adopted accordLng to how these two varlables interact. *
151. Such programs have been undertaken frequently by a broad coalltlon of
government, private enterprise, labor, financial and community interest groups.
They have sought to create a positlve atmosphere at an early stage of the
restructurLng process and a set of coammunity-based lnstltutions with a high
degree of local autonomy to provide advLce and resources---job centers, venture
capital firms, retraining and education agencies. Financial lncentives have beon
found to generally work best when they are provided up front, rather than over
an extended timetable whlch may risk dependence and high costs. In cases where
job losses are concentrated, clearly defined job creation strategLes have been
adopted. Smoe strategies emphasise the attractlon of new large businesses to the
area affected and SHE spinoffs, others focus more directly on the start-up of
small enterprise worksbops.
I52. Canada's Industrial Adjustment Service (IR) is an example of the
successful application of these concepts over an extended period of tlme. The
IAS has operated for over 20 years, recently concluding annually about 600-700
adjustment agreements (each agreement averages 1-2 years duration) covering a
total of 350,000-400,000 workers each year.   Nearly two-thirds of these
agreements deal with labor force layoffs or the threat of layoffs by enterprise.,
sector-wide adjustment, or community-level adjustment to restructuring by large
local industries. The IAS is used widely as it offers: (a) a proven systemic
process of assLsting a signLfLant volume of workers to find new employment
which, at the same time, tendb to improve management/labor relationas  (b) a
network of experienced private sector consultants to guide the program wlthin
each enterpriset and (c) unit costs which are lower than those the enterprise
would incur on Its own and funding of generally up to 50% of the costs of
assistance.
153. The lAB reports that, of the workers seeking s"sistance under URS layoff
agrements over the past three years, 75-82% found new positions (generally
wLthin three months) at a combined average cost to the Ia8 and private
enterprlses of les  than US$200 per person.  It is believed to reduce the
transitLon tim. between jobs and, consqyently, to cut finacial losses and
social stress at the individual, family and national levels as well as reduce
demands on public program lLke unamployment insurance.
154. The Government of Colombia has recently established a labor Adjustment
Assistance Program (LAIP) satisfactory to the Bank as part of the IRMP, in
particular to counterbalance steps anticipated in the labor regme to enable
increased mobility of workers as needed for industrial restructurLng. 'The
immediate objectives of the LP are to reduces

2.7 T chn2lM  Policy
161. The adoption of new technology will be an essential part of the
restructuring and development of many industries, as was illustrated in the
previous Chapter. The subsector studies have shown that technological renovation
is a process which is required all along the chain of manufacturing functions,
from product research and development to quality control, marketing and
management,  and that softwar  is as necessary as hardware.   The main
prerequisites for technological innovation, such as macroeconomic stability, a
relatively open external trade regime, and limlted restrictions on domestic
competition, are expected to be present in the coming years and to provide a
reasonably favorable environment for technology acquisition.
162. This environmeat needs to be complemented, however, by more explicit
foreign investment and technology transfer policies as, at least over the medium-
term, most technology requirements must be imported and as externalities often
result from the fact that innovators may be unable to capture all of the
associated rents. Also, economies of scale frequently exist under which smaller
firms may not be able to invest individually in th equipment, skills and efforts
required. And further, the financial risks (including frequently an absence of
oollateral) linked to the investigative, prototype and testing phases of
technology development may well exceed the thresholds of most financial
institutions. This section examines the key policy and institutional adjustments
recently made or to be made under the Project to support the acquisition,
adaptation and local creation of technology for industry.
(a) Foredan Direct Investment
163. In the 195034 the Colombian ,o4ernment pursued an industrial development
policy of import substitution in intermediate and capital goods largely via



-55-
public support (mainly ln steel, some basic chemicals and petroleum derlvatives)
and foreign direct investment (ID0). Over 1957-64, some WS$ 115 million in
regLstered PDX, accounting for about 20-30% of sector investment, went lnto paper
and cardboard, pharmaceuticals and other chemlcals, rubber products, metal
mechanlcal and transport aquLpment industrles.   Shese capLtal- and skill-
intensive ventures appeared at the time to have responded to the Government ' aim
to address gaps in capital formation, technology and production and management
skills for large plants. Zmployment ln those flrm with majority JDI also
increased over this period from 4 to 12% of total sector employment. "
164. The balance of payments crisis of 1966 led inter alia to the first policy
steps to contain the increasingly prominent FD1. Decre 444 of 1967 placed a 14%
ceiling on annual profit remittances as a share of investments of foreign firms
and prohibited investments in the financial sector.  A  part of the Andean
Group's efforts to coordinate industrial policieo, Decision 24 of 1970 reinforced
Decree 444 by prohibiting foreign monopoly power in key sectors, reducing maximum
foreign ownership to 49%, and eliminatLng restrictive practices between parent
and affiliate companies.
165. With the falling industrial output and international recession of the early
19808, the Government markedly revised FDT policy (Resolutions 29 and 36, and
Decree 3548 of 1983-84). This step reduced the protective element of FDI policy
and provided incentives aimed to target FD! toward national economic priorities.
in particular, it (a) increased profit remittances to 50% per year for priority
subsectors (mainly heavy industry and exports), (b) suspended obligations of 100%
IDI enterprises to form joint ventures where raw material content or exports
exceeded certain levels, (c) suspended geographical location restrictions on FDI,
and (d) expanded the possibility of reinvesting earnings.
166. Despite these regulatory changes, the focus of industrial FDI has remained
much the same since the late 195Os: highly capital and skill-intensive, with 57%
of cumulative investment in 1989 still concentrated in chemicals, paper, rubber,
plastics, electrical machinery and transport equipment. Over 1970-89, the real
value of cumulative registered PDI in industry rose very modestly, by a total of
about 13.9%, and accounted for less than 4% of gross fixed capital formation In
industry (Figure 2.2). However, FDI has played a prominent 'role in a number of
product groups and figure. among the most rapidly growing parts of Colombian
industry. Industrial FDS was dominant in 12 of 62 4-digit subsectors and, of the
85 largest industrial firm in Colombia in 1986, 36 were foreign. Over
1970-83, 11 of the 25 firms which were both the largest and fastest growing were
foreign. 14
167. The ID! regime was further liberalized in 1987 through fundamental
modifications in Decision 24 of the Andean PVct (Decision 220). Steps were taken
to (a) extend the period of transformation of FDI to majority local ownership
from 15-20 years to 30-37 year  and apply them only to firms seeking expanded
market shares; (b) eliminate share restrictions of FDI in national firm and the
7% limitation on reinvestment of prof its (C) eliminate regional restrictions on

using the technology proposed.

More importantly, even if the Superintendency Jad bean glven resources to carry

180. unvironAmetal Regulations and Zuforceaent. Whlle Colombia's industrial
pollution control laws are quite comprehensive, enforcement is generally weak and
fines (even when imposed) are often les than the cost of compliance.   Colombia
has two principal environmental laws. One is the Codigo Nacion&l de los Recurso
Naturales y Protecclon al Nedio Ambiente (Law 23 of 1973, Decree-Law 2811 of
1974, and subsequent decrees, all detailed by ortega, 1986), which covers a broad
range of environmental issues, including forestry, fisheries, wlldlife, and land
and water use. The other is the Codigo Sanitario Naclonal (Law 9 of 1979 and
Decree 1594 of 1984), which addresses pollution and related pub1lc health
concerns   While these laws are generally considered comprehensive and well-
drafted, they are only infrequently implemented. To a large extent, these two
laws have not yet been translated into enforceable regulations and operatlng
procedures for the Government agencies which must enforce them.
181. Industrial pollution control, most forest and watershed management, and
conservation program such as National Parks are the responsibility of IND3)WNa,
established in 1968 as the principal national Government agency responsible for
environmental and renewable resource management. The main functions of INDIRUNa
include (a) screening and approval of all major development projects, based on
environmental impact studies submitted by the implementing ministries or
agencies, and (b) long-term environmental monitoring of development projects
after they are built.   It operates under the aegis of the Ministry of
Agriculture, even though it is supposed to supervise and coordinate the
environment-related work of all the other ministries.
182  INDERENA is a notoriously weak institution which lacks the budget, staff,
political clout, and the continuity of leadership necessary for effectively
carrying any of its numetous responsibilities. In practice, many environmental
studies are submitted after construction, has begun or are simply not done.
Despite its logal mandate, INDIRENA has been unable to modify substantially, much
leos stop, most of these projets. INDERENA collects relatively little money
from timber concessions or water use fees and has virtually no access to any
"earmarked" funds (such as the Law 56 watershed management allocations from
electric power sales). As a result, it relies very heavily on fairly minimal
appropriations from the central Government Salaries within INDERENA are low by
Government standards, staff turnover is high, and morale is poor. Supplementing
INDMRXNA ars  18 different regional development  corporations, which  are
responsible for integrated natural resougo management and environmental
protection in their geographical areas of jA4iosdiction. As a rule, the larger



-60- .
and better-financed regional corporatLons tend to have better-developed capacity
for watershed and other natural resource management. Overall, however, they are
also weak.

(b) occunational Health and Safety
184. The Government has stated that safeguards in the work environment against

of Labor and Social Security established minimum regulations for occupational
health and safety in enterprises (Resolution 1016). They set out norms and
preventative programs to be gradually adopted in order to achieve quantifiable
reductions ln accidents, work-related health problems, abs6ntLsm, and increases
in enterprise productivity.  Investments are to be subject to cost-benefit
calculations, recognizing the need for firms to achieve and maintain adequate
returns.
185.  Each program at the enterprise -evel is to include activities in

systems, studies on the toxiclty of raw maierLals and processing substances used

operation and maintenance of equipment, investigation and analysis,pf accidents,

would be required and monitoring carried out by the Ministry." Under the Private

1986 for Ln. 1834-COO and Annex 3).
1,o. The experience gained in these projects has inspired more attention under
the Pifth SHE Project and the proposed Project to changes ln policy and
institutional factors, which are equally important constraints to efficient
industrial development as the lack of available credit. Careful analysia and
exhaustive discussions, partLcularly on the trado, fLnancial and labor regimes,
have been carried out. Consequently, the Project links its financing at the
enterprise level to a specific industrial policy framework and the achievement
of sector-wide adjustment measures to increase the competitiveness of the
business environment. in addition, unlike the earlier crodit lines, the Projcts
(a) emphasizes the increase of productive efficiancy as well as capacity
expansion of industry; (b) has fostered during Project preparation restructuring
initiatives by the private sector; (0) expands the range of exigible expeanditures
to be financed by the Bank's loan to meet restructuring investmnt requirements;
and (d) supports industrial subsector and restructuring service institutions to
help implement the restructuring process.
3.3   bjiective, Stratev and Beneficiaries
192. The development objective of the Project is to increase the international
competitiveness of Colombian industry. Its immediate objectives are to assist
the private sector -in:- rnovating existin  lndustrial production capacity so as
to reduce costs, building new capacity, increasing product quality and service,
and changing the production mix toward goods in which Colombia has a dynamli
comparative advantage, and phasing out production capacity which is unlikely to
produce acceptable long run returns in a competitive business environment. The
basic strategy to achieve these objectives is to adopt a set of public policy
adjustments which will increase the competitiveness of the domestic business
environment and to -enable enterprises to respond accordingly by making available
financial, technical and social instruments to help industrial enterprLses to
restructure and develop their operations.
193. The Project is expected to support some 250 investments by industrIal
enterprises over about a three-year period (CY91-93) - These are likely to be
distributed among a relatively limited number of larger investments and many
smaller investments by enterprises in light industry. These investments will aim
to. increase significantly industrial competitiveness and build clientele in
export markets through new products, reduced cost, improved product quality and
service. In the textile and clothing subsector, for example, the CG study
suggests that annual productivity increases could reach 6% and 2%, respectively,
with a substantial jump in employment in the clothing industry (47,000 workers,
see Table 3.1) and positive effects on the trade balance (V8$300 million net)
over a five-year adjustment period.  The Project would also facilitate the
redeployment of those in the industrial labor force displacee by the
restructuring -process,  promote improve health and safety standards within
enterprLses, and upgrade environmental protection.   The Government consiaers
that the Project represents a commitment to ta3e the first steps in a long-term
process of renovation and development of Colombia s industrial structure. If
successful, additional financing would be considered to help meet requirements
in succeeding years.

3.4 pDseri. tton
194. The Project would incorporate specific public policy adjustments critical
to the achievement of the IRDP. It would also includes (a) a private sector
credit program to finance invrastmente and technical assistance to Industrial
enterprises and supporting services, and (b) a public sector support program for
restructuring. An organization chart of the Project is on the following facing
page and a description of the Project is provided below.
Industrial Restructuring Policy Framework
195. -Public policies and regulatory adjustment  considered to be the most
essential for the success of the restructuring and development process (see
previous Chapter), have already been or would be made under the Project. A
matrix of adjustments under the Project is found at the end of this Chapter.
196. Trade. Under the Government's trade reform program lnitiated in February
1990, quantitative restrictions on manufacturing imports have been eliminated
(excluding those associated with health and safety). It has further announced
publicly final targets for the reforms by December 1993 the maximum tariff level
will not exceed 23%. the average import-weighted tariff will not exceed 16% and
the number of tariff categories will be reduced to no more than four. Ongoing
commitment of the Bank' a loan under the IRDP Credit Program would be conditioned
upon (i) maintenance of these three final targets, and (Li) coverage of-
industrial domestic production by quantitative restrictions not exceeding St of
value (excluding permits related to health an$ safety and national security) and
calculated in accordance with methods agreed upon with the Bank. This second
condition would not apply if, in the opinion of the Bank, any such increase above
the 5S limit is required exclusively as a temporary means to prevent or detain
a fall in the Government's international reserves caused by an extreme and
unexpected disequilibrium in its balance of payments and, further, that the
Government prepare within 120 days of the QR imposition, an action plan
satisfactory to the Bank for the substantial elimination of the QRs exempted from
the limitation and put it into effect within 180 days of the imposition.
Adjustments have already been made in the admiListration of the Plan Vallejo
drawback scheme to ensure that approval processing time does not exceed about 10
days for new applications and 2 days for changes.
197. Domestic Competition. As a condition of first disbursement to enterprises
in the respective subsectors, (a) price controls on domestic cotton production
(used in the textile and clothing industries ISIC 3211-3220) would be converted
to a price stabilization scheme, acceptable to the Bank which is closely linked
to international pricesi and (b) in the vehicle assembly industry (I8IC 3843),
the Government would adopt a deregulation program satisfactory to the Bank.
198. Financial Sector. The following steps have been taken to rationalize
interest rates and deregulate' direoted credit liness (a) eliminated remaining
interest rate subsidies on BR industrial credit lines (FIP, FFI and 1C0),
excepting for "frontier zones", for which a small differential is mandated by law
and for enterprises which are victims of narcoterrorism (subloan authorizing
closing date is April 1991 for this facility)l (b) raised the maximum lending
rates to enterprises to DTF +6 percentage pointst and (c) harmonized PIP and F7$

199. Labor. Revisions to current labor legislation and regulations have been
approved by Congress which (a) introduce competitiveness among the "just cause
criteria for transfer and dismissal of employees, adoption of new technology, and
the reduction of closing of production capacity; (b) provide for more flexible
labor contracts according to competitiveness needs, (¢) suspend the "unit
enterprise" concept now constraining differentiation of labor costs, and (d)
reduce the time required to make decisions regarding the mobilLty of labor and
changes in enterprise organisation.
200. Technology. Measures have been taken to substantially convert, the criteria
and review process applied to technology and technical services contracts by the
Superintendency of Industry into -a passive registry system,7 and reduce thQ
processing time for applications.
Private Sector Credit Program (total cost, US$492 million)
201. Definition. The Credit Program (summarized in Annex 4) would wupport
investments by enterprises qualifying as (a) manufacturing and mLning sector
businesses, and (b) service institutions helping prepare and implement
investments by such sector businesses, including those oriented to the industrial
subsector level (eg., product design, technology and export promotion centers).
Investments would be defined as those almed at developing or sustaining the
international competitiveness of existing- or new enterprises. The Project would
finance loans by participating financial intermediaries (P115) and quasi-equity
and equity contributions by PFle, other legally established entities and
individuals to enterprises qualified under the Project. The program would also
finance an Investment Preparation Pacility to meet the preinvestment costp
incurred by enterprises of preparing sound investment proposals. Advances under
this FacliLty would be either rescheduled by the investment loan or recovered
within a period of three years from the date of the advance.
202. Xligible expenitures to be financed by the Loan would be defined
negatively so as to exclude the financing by the Bank loan of a transfer of
assets, land and severance payments, and the refinancing of existing
indebtedness. Such expenditures would be most likely to include goods and
services associated with the acquisition of traditLonal fixed assets for future
working capital (including used fixed assets, for which there is an ample
competitive market and important reductions in barriers to market entry for
smaller firms) and a broad range of other incremental expenditures commonly
required in restructuring exercises such as product research and development,
\ 



-66-.
development of new marketing and distribution networks, employee retraining and
relocation and investment banking fees.
203. Structure.  All development banks (CF), including ZVI, and commercial
banks legally constituted in Colombia would, in principle, be eligible as PFIs
of the Project (Annex 5). Interested intermediaries would be qualified as Pus
upon satisfactory review by BR and the 'Bank 'of the following eligibility
criteria 
(a)   certification by the ,8uperintendency of Blanks that it is in good
standing with respect to its requirements for overall operating
policies and practices, financial condition and reporting.  The
standards.of the superintendency are,satisfactory to the Bank for
the purposes of the proposed Project; and
(b)  the existence of a valid participation Agreement.
SR would review semi-annually a PFIs organization, staffing, credit portfolio
performance, loan appraisal and supervision procedures for lending   d equity
contributions under the Project, thereafter maintaining or adjusting accordingly
free limits applicable to subloans.
204. Bach PFI would conclude a Participation Agreement with.BR to protect the
rights of the borrower and the Bank and to enabXe obligations to be carried out
per the Loan Agreement. The Participation ltgreement would also ensure the
effective separation between BR's role and second-tier manager of credit lines
and its traditional role as a central bank. It would further specify the need
to maintain an adequate overall financial condition, employ an adequate number
of staff capable of managing the portfolio of Project resources, and ensure
proper control and provisions for arrears. 'The terms and conditions of the
credit Program would be identical for all PF1..
205. *Istitutional Development. The Project would support the cost of technical
asistance to PFil, in particular to the. seven newly qualified CFs and
participating commercial banks, in the appraisal and supervision of investment
loans.  It would also support technical assistance services, semAhars and
promotional efforts by CFs to develop and expand their investment banking
services provided for industrial restructuring. Given that lending of Project
resources is not expected to exceed 2-30 of total new loan commitments of
commercial banking institutions over the commitment period, no additional
institutional performance conditionality beyond the requirements of the
Superintendency of Banks is warranted.

3 6 Relendin  Terms and Conditions
208. The proposed Bank loan would be made to BR at the standard variable rate
with the guarantee of the Republic of Colombia. BR would pay the standard Bank
commltment fee, and bear the explicit risks of interest rate and foreign exchange
fluctuations when relending in Pesos; when lending ts denominated in US$
equivalents, it would bear the explicit cross-currenci risk.  The loan would be
repaid in equal principal installments over 17 years, including a five-year grace
period. Any surplus of repayments from IRDP loans made by the Project's PFIo to
BR over amounts due to the Bank by BR would be recycled to PIle per the Project's
legal agreements.
209  BR would denominate the proceeds of the Bank's loan for the Credit Program
in Pesos or US$ equivalent, according to the preferences of the enterprise. A
,maximum amount per enterprise would be set by BR at no less than US$25 million
and could be raised at its discretion. BR would rediscount 70% of a subloan
denominated in Pesos made available by a Pr! to finance an enterprise investment
and charge an interest rate to the PFI equivalent to at least DTF plus a margin
to cover administrative cost and forelgn exchang risk. If the proceeds are
denominated in US$ equivalent, BR would rediscount 100% of the US$ denominated
subloans up to a maximum of 50% of total sub-project cost and charge a rate
reflecting the Bank's interest rate plus a fee to cover the cross-currency risk
and BR's administrative costs. The Government is expected to take steps shortly
which would enable the private sector to hedge the Peso-US Dollar exchange risks
through private institutions. BR would bear the full foreign exchange risk on
Peso denominated subloans and only the cross-currency risk on US$ denominated
subloans.   Maturities of funds relent to PPls would match those set for
beneficiaries.



-68-
210. PFIs would set interest rates to clients according to the Lnvestment
characteristics, maturity, credit risk, procssing cost and competition for each
subloan up to a maximum rate of 6 percentage points above the rate from BR to the
PFI, which would equal or exceed the average free market spreads and is currently
nearly S percentage points (in effective term) above the rates charged under the
Bank's previous credit line to Colombian industrialists. The maintenance of an
interest rate ceiling recognizes the continued oligopolistic structure of local
financial markets.  The Government expects, however, that   the degree of
competition in the sector will increase as its financial sector reforms proceed,
so as to permit by 1994 the rates to clients to be freely set by the PFI. The
long-term maturities of the Bank's loan would permit PFIs to compensate higher
interest rates with longer maturities when necessary to meet cash flow
requirements. - Loan maturities and grace periods for subloans and equity
financing would be set by the PF? according to the cash flow and other
characteristics of the investment up to a maximum of 10 years of maturity and 3
years of grace. PFIs would mobilize the remaining 30% of the subloan from their
own sources on terms identical to the rediscounted portion. Financing under the
Investment Preparation Facility would be repaid or rescheduled to match the
investment loan within three years.
211. The Project would also finance up to 70% of equlty investments by a
development bank or individual in a qualifying subproject. The investor and
enterprises undertaking the subproject would, as for a subloan, be required to
mobilize the remainder as safeguards to ensure that the entrepr  neur's commitmeut
and risk-taking function are preserved. The increase in the generally low
equity base of the sector (se previous Chapter) is a fundamental requirement in
order for it to face the increasing import competition generated by the trade
reform process and the additional risk involved in the industrial restructuring
process and the export market.
212. It would be expected that the fresh resources injected from the Credit
Program would be provided frequently in conjunction with debt rescheduling and
ref inancing, debt/equity conversions and/or write-of fe of existing loans of PFIs
to help to ensure an appropriate enterprise capital structure with which to carry
out the investment. Approval of an enterprise loan would require normally at
least a 12% financial rate of return in real terms supported by a senitivity
analysis. Given that the current trade reform program should substantially
reduce the distortions in the economy, an economic rate of return would not be
required.
213. Bank loan funds for the Public Sector support Program would be channeled
through BR to the Government and repaid by the Governent on terms identical to
those between the Bank and BR.

222. The proposed Project would help Colombla to reallgn lndustrlal production

lnvestments whlch reduce product costs, improve quallty and servlce, and

223. However, several Project implementatlon risks are present. Reversals may
occur in the Government's trade reform program. To mitigate this rlsk, the
program has been publlcally announced, is well underway and the Project includes
safeguards whlih would tle the Bank's Loan to the malatenance of the trade
program's progress. secondly, Pils may be reluctant to flnance the hlgher credit
risks inherent ln lnvestments.  To deal wlth thls eventuallty, the Project
provides that Pile may set margins accordLng to credit rLsk up to a maximum
approximating free market rates.
224. And thlrd, the frequent complexlty of restructuring lnvestments may result
In the financing of inadequately prepared projects. To mitigate this rlsk,
subsector analyses nd restructuring strategLes were prepared during Project
preparation for five Ladustries in part as a demonstration effect for the sector
at large. An aglle pro-investment flnanclng mechanism has also been Lncluded in
the Project to finance adequate consulting and lnvestment bankng services to
anterprlse in order to prepare Lnvestments.

1.          Two product groups in the agroindustry sector were initially selected
by the Ministry of Econosic Development for intensive study and analyslss fruits
and vegetables, fluid milk and mllk products. other agroindustry activities that
are suitable for restructuring analysis include aquaculture, seat processing, and
the packaging industrles.
5. 8tructure and Performance
2.          The processing industry is principally comprised of small and medium-
size firms engaged in diverse product lines.  The larger-size firms are
concentrated in a more limited product range including primarily tomato sauce,
juices of various kinds, and soups. A recent survey ldentified ome 54 principal
F&V prccessors including a core group of about 25 well-established companies.
However, five processors shared 93 percent of the total value of production
during 1981-86, a high concentration in this subqroup.
3.          On the basis of a representative sample survey by DANE, the F&V
processing group showed a negative growth rate of -9.7 percent over the 1980-86
period. In 1980, the FY&V group (ISIC group 3113) represented 2.0S percent of the
value added of the food manufacturing sector (major group 311-312) but this share
had declined to 0.49 percent in 1986. I nvestments in plant and equipment in 1986
were also reported to be at the same level as in 1980. Since 1986, however, new
processing operations have come into existence in the fields of fruit juices,
pulp and concentrates for the export market. Characteristically, the industrial
plants are concentrated in or near to the principal urban aras.  The survey
places 22 processors in Bogota and Cundinamarca, 3 in Manizales, 2 in Pereira,
12 in Medellin, 10 in Cali-Valle, 4 in Barranquilla and 1 in Bucaramanga.
Proximity to market, available infrastructure, acceptable living conditions for
workers and management -- all have a role ln plant site selectior.
4.          A characteristic of the industry, also derived from the sample
survey, is the low level of backward integration to farm production. Only two
of the 24 firms surveyed participated directly in primary production of farm raw
materials. Generally speaking, only the largest lovcessors in a limited number
of products, e.g., tomato, peas, young corn, e*.rcise special dealings with
growers including contract purchases at pre-established prices, working capital
advances, technical assistance and post-harvest crop collection. It may be
mentioned that certain crops (e.g., industrial variety tomatoes) demand a specLal
relationship between grower and processor.   The crop would not be grown
otherwise, since no viable alternative market exists.                      1
5.  L       For comparison purposes, processed foods may be divided between
traditional products, their form and packaging, and more recent emerging products



-139-
Page 2 of 41
and processing technology.   In the first gxoup -- tomato sauce, pickles,
asparagus, peas, beans, pineapple, peaches, jams and marmlades, preserved or
dried and packaged in cans, bottles, jars, plastic - production reacbhd 20,000
tons in 1986 valued at 5,170 million pesos almost the same level of production
as 1980. However, more than 70 percent of production was dominated by three
products -- tomato sauce (38%), marmalade (225%) and peas (10.8%). On the other
hand, non-traditional export-oriented products including dried tropical fruits,
frozen fruit pulp, fruit juices and concentrates reached a production level of
17,700 tons in 1986 valued at 1,800 million pesos. The rate of growth over the
1980-86 period was an impressive 21 percent.
Dar
6.         The dairy industry is defined as the production of crude or raw-milk,
pasteurized milk, milk powder or powdered milk, and milk products such as cheee,
butter, yogurt and ice cream. In real terms, the value of production grew an
average 3.7 percent in the' period 1971-1986 although with sharp priods of
decline and growth influenced to a large degree by changes in GOC price policies
and regulations. Price controls over liquid iLlk also stimulated the rapid
growth of sub-products, particularly cheese.   In 1988, pasteurized ailk
represented 39.9 percent of liquid milk usage, sub-products 37.9 percent, crude
milk 15.3 percent and powdered mllk 6.9 percent. 'n 1980, Colombia imported 5 8
percent of their dairy requir _ nts. Zn 1988, c ficial imports were nil.
7.       - According to a 1986 DAS survey, there were 97 registerd dairy
producers in the country, or 8.5 percent of all registered companies in the food
industry, based on 10 employees or more. Direct mployment exceeded 8,000
persons.  Net profits of the industry declined considerably during the early
19808 due in part to higher financial costs and taxes. A recent sampling
indicates, however, strong interest in new investments tn equipment, processing
machinery, and transport.
8.         Crude milk production increased an average 6.1 percent during the
1980-88 period, far exceeding population growth rate of 2.2 percent.  This
impressive growth is attributed to a combination of factors:   (a) the
transformation of the cattle herd from predominant beef cattle to dual purpose
animals ("double proposito') -- for beef and dairy production, (b) advances .n
genetic breeding, (c) improved pastures, (d) more widespread use of feed
concentrates, (e) improved collection and storage of raw milk.
9.         With the exception of several multinational-owned powdered milk
establishments, the dairy industry is preddminantly coiombian-owned. Installed
capacity for paateurized milk is about five million liters daily based on one
shift operations, but utilized capacity is estimated at about 55 percent overall
but higher during the heavy far production seaeons. Approximately 50 percent of
pasteurization capacity is located int eh Bogota region. Mine companies share
38 percent of national capacity including the largest who has 10 percent of total
installed capacity.
10.        Powdered milk production is concentrated in even fewer hands. tour
companies make up 78 percent of installed capacity in the country. Although
installed capacity is substantially underutilized during most of the year, it is



-140.
Page 3 of 41
still generally insuffioient to hanle and process the large surplus available
during the heavy mil producng seaons.
11.        During the 1980-89 period, consumption pf pasteurLsed mik showed an
impresslve aerage annual rate of growth of 12.5 percent. Milk products grw by
5.2 percent but crude milk and industrLalized milk fell 3.4 and 4.7 p       t
respectively. Per caplta fluid .llk consumption is estimated at 57.5 liters/year
which is the highest in Latin America.

Fruits and Vjaetables
12.        Colombia enjoys the advantage of a variety of micro-climatic
conditions which has allowed the cultivation of about 34 types of vegetables and
29 different fruits -  with few exceptions, all are orieated to the domestic'
market. The potential for carving a niche in foreign madkets has given rise to
an intensive program, led by PROXmPO in conjundtion with PBCAI3 and SCA, to
enhance the development of selected fruits using modern cultivation practices,
technology and inputs. By 1988, the program had encompassed 14 fruLt types on
8,200 hectares or 25 percent of the total fruit planted area in the country.
13.        A major impediment to development of fresh frult exports is the
presence of the Mediterranean fruit fly in the country, and the banning of,
imports into Florida (major transhipment area). Consequently, Colombia has
focused their attention on the IMC market which now absorbs about 70 percent of
the country's fresh fruit exports. On the other hand, geographic diatance alon
would seem to militate against any substantial exports of freh vegetables to.
turope. The US appears the natural market for winter vegetables and high value
crops such as broccoli and asparagus.
14.        adoptive research of different varieties of vegetables and trals of
post-harvest treatment appear to be lacking. Dissemination of information to the
agricultural community has been limited.  On the public 'sector side, this
responsibility has rested almost entirely with Ica.
15.        Processors identify limited raw materials Supply and erratic qualLty
as major problems confronting the industry since the procesesng industry is
competing with the fresh market.   AA extension of the PROXPO/FZDXCAU*
diversification program to the processing industry would sem t logical step to
stimulate the development of selected crops and products. Post-harvest losses
for some crops such as mango and avocado appear exceptionally high at 30 porcent
and 26 percent respectively.
16.        Custome duties of 25 percent or more on imported machinery and
equipment are factors in investment plans for modernization of factories.  tn
addition, investors must contend with an 18 percent surtax oni the CIF value of
imports and a sales tax of 10 percent calculated on the CIXr value plus the
custome duties and surtax.

20.        TransportatLon is a principal area of concern for export-orLented
busLnesses.   For perlhables, this means the availablILty of suffLcient
refrigerated containers,during the harvest seasons. Ocean freLght rates appear

exporters universally complain that 'bureaucratic" management of port facilities
by. the GOC entity 'COLPU3RT0" has resulted in higher shipment and handling costs
as well as delays in port.   Shipments by air from  Bogota to Europe are
constrained by the limited flights and space available. In thls regard, fresh
fruLts must compete with cut-flowers whL.h pay a 70 percent higher air freight
rate. Lastly, the country lacks a container port torminal and heavy lift
equLpmnt for on-loading of container cargo on ocean-goLng contaLner vesels.
21.        The dairy lndustry ln Colombia, as in most developing countrles, is
dometLc-oriented.  Seldom are sufficient surpluses generated to foru the
foundation for an export lndustry.  The dairy product most heavily traded
lnteraationally is milk powder. World commorce in 1980 represented 60 percent
of world production.   For Colombia, the sole product with any chance of
generating a level of export earnings is also milk powder. Local prices however,
are eatrmated to exceed international prlces by about 23 percent.
.22.       The gaps in international competitiveness are both internal and
external. The competitive position of .the lndustry, from the intirn4A side,
reflects the stream of activlties starting froc cattle productlon u- Ito GO¢
polLeLos affectLag farm production and industrial processing.  It would be
foolish, however, to  cite  basic  or long-term  shortcoings as gape to
international competltiveneas.  8Liply stated, Col1mbla doe not enjoy any
inherent comparative advantage ln dairy products. Externally, as long as the SEC

D. Domestic and Internationmal Marke Propat
Fruits and Vectable
23.         There has been a declded lncrease ln demand for fresh fruits,
vegetables and juLces in the NBC and the U.S. ainee 1980. On a lower scale, the
market in Japan also has shown subotantLal gains. Changing consumer tastes,
increased health and dLetary concerns, growaing consumptLon of foods outeLde the
home have contributed to the growLng demand for fresh frult and vegetable
products.
24.         The ESC market, the largest saigle market in the world for fresh
fruit  and vegetables, presents  speclal problem   for ColombLa.    Strong
protectionist measures, internal subsLdLes, the Lome convention, Spain's entry
into the EEC, tridltlonal suppliers in the Sahara and Middle East rim,
comparative disadvantages ln transportation -- all mliltate against the
development of a dynamic trade to the EEC ln traditLonal fresh fruits and
vegetables. Righ value tropical fruits would have a better chance, either by
fast ocean transportation or air freLght - already evident in the present
direction of trade -- as pointed out, 70 percent of ColombLa's frult exports go
to Europe.
25.         in Japan, Colombla is competing with Paciflc rlm countries for
tropical fruits (Philippine exports of plneapple and bananas to Japan in 1987
exceeded US$73 mllion) and vegetables (over USS70 mllion in 1987 from Northt and
South Xorea and China) at well as the U.S., Brazil, Mexico and other countries
for juices and vegetables.
26.         The US "winter vegetable" market would seem to offer the best
opportunity for ColombLa in the fresh vegetable trade. Although the US is free
of protectlonlst measures, Colombia must reach out to higher and sustainable
standards of quality, and improve and develop the linkages in the agribusiness
chaln -- selectlon of seed varieties, adoptlve research, cultivation practices
and technology, post harvest treatment, storage, transportatlon to port, ocean
transportatLon and U.S. marketing. An important element in thls trade would be
the development of close ties wlth U.S. importers/marketers, many of whom are
active and providing technical assistance to growers ln competina
Caribbean/Mexico/Central Amerlcan countries. The presence of the Mediterranean
frult fly La also a detrLment to expand trade from Colombla to the U.S. for frssh
fruits and further investigation of thls Lisue seems in order.
27.         on the processed aide, Colombia's best opportunities Ile in frozen
fruits and vegetables and in juLce concentrates.  In the U.8., frozen vegetable
consumptlon has contlnued to rtie ln contrast to the decline ln canned
vegetables. on the U.S. import sLde, frozen broccoll and frozen cauliflower
dominate the lit, but much is attributed to U.S. multinational investment
operations. The j-.ice trade has shown dynamlc growth over recent years including
non-traditional troplcal frults such as papaya, mango, guava and passion fruit.

29.         The Colombia dairy lndustry, as expected, La focused on the domestic

The dairy ladustry ln Colcmbla, as in most developLng countrLes in the tropical

with international prLces, a relaxation of CCC prlce regulatLons might facillitate

Another alternative put forward is the channeling of lower priced surplu crude

30.         Export-oriented n&V products, both fresh and processed, are at an
LneLpient stage of development. Many weaknegses in the food production and
marketing chaLn are evident, and point to the need for various actions In order
to substantLally improve the efficiency, competitiveness, export and domestic
potential of the 1x group of products.  The proposed restructuring strategy
encompasses four principal comp_onns as described belows
I.    Ao1matuan  rQiaetXiga

15.       The existing cotton pricing and marketing policy in Colombia is
irrational. The domestic prSce of raw cotton is, lsmost always, higher than the
international price of equivalent cotton. since cotton is the most used raw
material for the Colombian textile industry, and lt accounts for nearly 50% of
yarn and 3S% of fabric cost, the price of raw cotton is a major detetminant of
the export competitiveness of the industry. The Governeant s cotton prLcing
policy needs to be reviewed. Furthermore, the present cotton grading mechanism
is deficient. The price of cotton, particularly the short-stapled, need to be
determined in line with quality and grade. It appears that Colombian textlle
industry has a competitive edge in exporting coar_s-count yarns provided the
price of raw material is right.
16.         The existing labor laws have made it difficult for the textile
industry to rationalize its labor force and adapt new technologLes.
Consequently, the industry is carrying about 20% excess labor than required for
its operations. It is particularly hard for the older, large-sized enterprwies
to rationalize labor. Any restructuring effort for the industry must include iA
the agenda a thorough review of the labor legislation, and wage structure.
17.         The  Colombian  textile  industry  'i  characterized  by  heavier
concentration of installed capacity in a few plants, and a fragmented capacity
in a large number of uneconomic-sized plants. The typical exampl is the
knitting and garmntin  subsector. Despite being competitive, it is unable to
show a better export performance due to its inability to accept large export

orders, gLven a highly fragmented and dLiorganized setup. It appears that there

18.        A very significant portion of the spinning, weaving, and fabric
finlihing capacity is run-down, low productive, and qualLty ineffLcLent. The
preparatory equlpment ln spLnning and weaving need to be updated. All the ring
frames beyond 15 year age, need to be replaced with updated equLpment.   The
lndustry is carryLng too many lneffclient, narrow-width weaving looms. There is
a need to replace these with the wide-width, state-of-the-art shuttleless looms.
simultaneously, cone winding should be updated with yarn splLers. Finishing
plants are, by and large, discontinuous, batch-type, and inefficient In term of
consumption of dyes/chemicals. These plants need to be updated with continuous
bleaching, and dyeing (thermosol). With rehabLlitation the sector could become
cost effectlve, and develop export competitiveness.
19.        Given the steady increase in polyester flber/filament consumption,
this reasonably efficlent subsector could be expanded. On the other hand, the
nylon manufacturing capacity in the country seems to have exceeded the potential
demand.
20.        Export marketing management for the sector in place, is deficient.
Most enterprises, due to their siLes and the costs involved, can not afford an
independent export marketing set up, and hence do not have access to information
on international market trends. Creation of an effective centralized marketing
setup, to aasiLt the entire sector in export marketing efforts, with one-time
governmental support is necessary. The running cost of the set-up could be met
through contrLbutions from the industry.
21.        Absence of a centralLsed Research and QualLty-control set-up for the
size of the industry, as in Colombia, is a major surprLe. This explains the low
concern for quality in a large number of smaller plants, which cannot afford an
independent quality control and maintenance set-ups.

1.         The leather and shoe Lndustry in Colombia is very modest in miss,
representing under 5S of total value added of the manufacturLng setor La 1988.
It is compoed of five distinct semntes  loughtorhous (moe thAn 1000,
generallyy-small units blonging to municipal gov), marketin  agents
(about 100, which collect, store, tranprt md finan   hidos/finished leather
to/from the tanng idustry), tanneries, (over 540, of which 4 have about 43%
of the market), and manufacturers of shoe and leatbhr goods (largely luggage,
handbags, and work gloves). The shoe industry is fragmented Into about 2,650
shoe producer firms, of which some 2453 (93%) are microenterprLses. output is
heavily concentrated, however, with 12 establisbmnts accounting  for 61% of
subsector sales. In the leather Industry, 7 firm (8% of the total) produce 63%
of the output. Production is also concentrate gelly in Colombia's four
major urban areas, whe  77% of the output for leather and S9% for shoes is
located.
2.         Production of the subsector tends to be drLven maly by chan  in
demographic conditions and per capita consmption. xt was substantially affected
by the recessionary years of the 1980e, though it has recovered steadily sine
1984 with growth of about 5t per year.  Still, production in 1988 rere     d
no more than about 60% of instaled capcity. The mrket is primarily supplied
by local producers, though contraband imports of shoes are estimated to be 18%
of apparent dostic consumption. Casual street shuo  and sport shoes tend to
account for most of the market (38% and 27%,respectively),  followed by sandals
and work shoes. Competition within the domestic market appears strong, though
more than half of the shoes being purchased are throgh retail outlets which ar
vertically  tegratd with their producer.
3.         With local market growth potential not likely to substantially
surpass demgraphic trends, the export market i  an important alternative now
being examined by many local producers. Subsetor exports represented about 6%
of total shoe sales in 1988 (Ipriarily leather work shoe), plus another 15%
indirectly through local sale  to foreign tourists.  Bxport quadrupled over
1985-88, reilching US$137 million in that year and exceeding Colombials overall
industrlal export trend. about one-half went to the US market while another 42%
went to other developLng countries (particularly Venesuela, Panama, 2cuador, the
Dominican Republic and Singpore.) Colombials main competitors in these markets
are from Brafl, Argentlna, Taiwan and Mexico.
4.         Raw materials aocount for betwee 52-66% of the total cost of shoe
And leather goods products, followed by labor costs of 16-18.5%. In 1988, The
sbsector mploye directly about 53,000 peron  (about 12%- of all industrial
mployment), of which the sho  industry accounts for about 39,000 pero   and
leather goods some 7,200. Lbout one-third wee employed b y large sale fLr=,
one-third by   arontri,nd the remainder Ln smal and meum scale
enterprises.  Production is labor-intensive, reqLrin  more that double the

B. InternatJ9lonal tUtMvepffs
5.         The leather and shoe industry  in -Vlombia has two inherent
competitive advantages bearing on price and service - its proximLty to the large
export markets of North America and generally competitive prices of domestic
hides (US$0.65 /ft2 in Colombia vs. US$0.82 in Brazil for raw hides). The
industry has also developed substantial installed capacity and experience in its
domstic market. However, several important factors have inhibited the growth
of major segments of the industrys
(a)  the very limited access to high quality raw mateialas (mainly
hides);
(b)   the  relatively  demanding  requiremenw,  for  capital-intenive
processes and chemical technology of tanneries;
(a)  limited incentives for growth in a saturated domestic market and the
impact of contraband; and
(d)  the demanding standards in external market for hlgh quality, style,
and its rapid turnover, which requlre relatively sophisticated
production technologies and materials.
The following paragraphs highlight thes and other factors which influence the
international competitiveness of the industry.
6.          Domestic Rr MaterilX  amn the Buoolv            Thstem   The basic leather
reaching manufacturers in the industry, while lower than comparable international
prices, generally is  of poor quality.   Print, the most abundant Colombian
livestock is the Sebu which, while well-suited to tropical climates, has an
anatomy and skin quality which does not produce high quality leather.  in
addition, parasites and animal activities tend to leave abundant visible scars
on finished leather. Hides ar further damaged by livestock handling practices
by rancher and slaughterhouses, including from barbed wire fencing, branding on
the best sections of the skLn, ivpooper herding and trucking, inefficient
skinning causing a loss of usable area, and slow treatment of skins which cause
deterioration in leather quality.
7.         Whle much of the intermedLation of raw hides and fLnished leather
operates in a competitive evionment, it is fragmented and froequntly a  low
process. Colobla Is cattle herds are spread relatively widety across the country
and are linked with a very inadequate transport network. This results in a
decentralized network of slaughterhouses and complicated supply of skins to
tanneries. Intermediation is further complicated by the geographical dispersion
of producers of leather goods and shoes, which requLre a wide range of supplies

8.         !port.d_ Production Inguta and the Trade ReaLme.  imports of other
production inputs, such as equipAent and spare parts, requires 4-5 month from
arrival at the ports in Colombla and demands the malntenanoe of high inventories

thLs import regime disadvantages the cost and quality of producta ln thli

responses by producers to remain competitive.  in additLon, the incentives
provided by CERTS and Plan Vallejo are, according to the industry, both unstable
and strongly miLtlgated by the combined costs of high importer input inventories,
delays in Government trade institutions, and bankLng operations. Accordingly,
a 12% CURT is estimated to have a net value of 5-8%.
9.         JanneieL2.  The processing cost of Colombian tanneries is high
(US$0.83) in relation to comparator countries such as Brazil (US$0.71), and
Taiwan (US$0.59). Without restructuring, they cannot compete with ssmi-finished
imported loather. The large tanneries produce acceptable quality leather from
Cenerally poor skins. The amaller tanneriles lack the equipment and technology
do so, resulting frequently in mediocre quality leather. all operate profitably,
however, glven the relatively low cost of raw hides. In general, servLce time
of the tanneries is slow glven poor management and operations. for exports of
leather and shoes, this problem is compounded by slow transport modes and
substantial Government bureaucracy.
10.        $hoe an  Leather gods  rodutction.   Production technology is
frequently outdated and has a sigaficant negatlve lnfluence on product cost and
qualLty. Overall productivlty in relating to the optimum for the installed
capacity of the leather and shoe industry is relatively low and is largely a
consequence of:
(a)  an excessive number of product lines which reduce the beneflts
gaLned from economLes of scale and newer technologies, and slow
delivery and servLea capacity;
(b)  inadeqate industrial engineering, partLcularly to optimize plant
layout, manage materials, and qualLty controll
(c)  unrellable and costly supply of certain key chemlials, such as
sulfuric, caustic soda and bicarbonate, whlch are presently produced
by a Government monopoly lndustry and subject to interruptions in
supply due largely to problems in labor relationst and
(d)  inadequate working conditions, personnel manage mt and on-the-job

of SURA in this area is observed to not be well suited to thes
specialized trainlng requLred.

orientation toward partloular market niches, detracting from economies of scale.

from emerging "next generation" producers in other developing countries.

external and internal markets, and fasteners and zippers need to be better
adapted to external market styles.

13.        The long-term trend in global productlon of shoes continues to be
increasingly export-oriented, with its location continuing to shift toward
economically developing countries. They accounted for 67% of total production
in 1976 and 77% in 1986. At the oam time, now sources of produetion within that
group of countries appear, as in China, Indonesia and Thailand, as labor costs
rise in countries like Taiwan and Korea. In addition, the product mix is also
shifting more toward sport shoes.

and BrazLl.

D. 1ndicativ  Restruaturing Mg  evlouent Plan
16.        With a concerted restructuring effort, Colombia's shoo an  leather
goods industry could successfully compete ln the Increasingly competitive
domestic market which will result from trade reform and in external markets. The
international market offers the greater growth potentlal and both shoe and
leather goods producers seek to increase their orientation toward it. The shoe
industry La likely to be most competitive internationally in street shoes of
casual styles and with a high leather content, particularly those shoes and boots
with leather soles. Restructuring is considered to be critical in the areas
discused below.
17.        The quality of domestic bldes should be improved, inter alia with the
development of a classification system to grade quality and reflect dlfferentials
in their prices. A price differential of 12-15% should be adequate to stimulate
the development and provision of higher quality hides. in parallel, tannerie
neod to increase their orientation toward the higher quality and service
standards for fLniheOd leather demnded by the export market.   Capacity
utilization must be raisd from its currently low 43% by paying more attention
to materials management (better skinning practices can yield up to 70% of the
hide usable rather than the present 40%), improving the availability of imported
inputs, upgrading equipmeAt in order to improve leather quality and reduce
processing time, upgrading chemical technology, and better control recycling of
damaged hides. Greater specialization in types of leather rather that the
present production of a great variety by each firm, would also permit larger
production runs and better penetration of specific market segments. Investments
to reduce environmental pollution and financial restructuring to reduce fLnancial
coats are also needed.
I8.        At the enterprise level, greater product specialization is essential
to take advantage of economies of scale and newer te*hnologie. Industrial
engineering advisory ervices are ne4ede to increase plant efficlency and
training of the work force. Some consolidation of installed capacity among
tanneries is likely to be n"cssary and may also be required in the hoe industry
if growth rates do not take up existing slack capacity. At the subsector level,
the industry could benefit from the development of a computer assisted design
(CAD) center for shoe deign and to better track export market trends. Close
attention neede to be paid to the size, segmeatatLon, competitLon and overall
trends in such markets, as well as distribution channels and packaging. Such
centers could also house the development of standards for sizes and product
quality, as well as be associated with training, technology and extension servicoe
centers to enterprises in the country.
19.        Thes steps should be accompanied by access under the trade system
to imported hides which would be required in the shorter run to meet production
needs, lower tariffs for prtduction inputs as well as progressiv reductions of
final goods protection, and to dedliated facilities for fashion-sensitive exports
in airport facilities.   It is estimated that, within the coming five years, a
successful restructuring program ensompassig all of these easOures could
increase real value added by over one-quarter its 1988 level, increase its export
orientation from about e-quarter to one-half of total production and generate
about 8,000 new direct jobs.

7         The technology used by the integrated producer APDR is relatively
inefficient. The blast furnace and basic oxygen furnaces are of relatively small
sixe as compared to modern standards, the casting process depends upon the
relatively inefficient ingot casting/blooming slabbing mill process, and flat
products rolling capacities are smaller than in efficient world practice By
world standards, APDR is oparating under several disadvantages, with relatively
few offsetting advantages.  The raw materials are of relatively low grade
requiring complex mLning and preliminary treatment processes, though close
proximity limits the need for extensive transport; these raw material do produce
a good grade of pig iron. This material is poorly transformed using the ingot
casting-primary and secondary rolling technologies, which lmpose a penalty ln
material yields, energy consumption oad labor utilization. And finally, the age
of existing equipment make production to current world quality levels extremely
difficult.

progressively updated by continuous investments mainly ia the areas of steel
making and continuous casting, to implement well established Lndustry technology.
