Asgtmbly Policg XCavork
1.   The auto industry in Colombia was deeloped through an approach conslstet
with those of a number of other developing countries durLng the 1960s and 1970m.
The objective -was to use the induttry as -a drLver of new .echnological
development for other important industries-metal mechanlc, steel, electronico,
etc., and as a source of emloyment. It was anticipated that, in time, domestic
suppllers of autoparts would develop to add local added value to the fLl
assembly process as well as necessary spare and replacement parts.
2.   The development of the domestic industry was based on agrements with
tranonatlonal auto producers to establish assembly operations in the country
bmsed on the importation of knock down kits (C0Da) under favorable tariff rates,
while the importation of completely bullt up unlts (CSUs) was largely excluded
through a combination of liconsing, quantltative restrictions (Oft) and high
tariff barriers.. The autoparts producrs were also protected by. high t-trff
barriers, domestic content or "must buy" lists, and exclusion of assemblers and
foreign autoparts producers from the autoparts subsector.  GVesrne nt policy
further spelled out the rules of the game in tetm of the number of different
lines and models that could be produced by the assemblers, foreign exchange and
other budgetary targets, training requirements for auto parts producers, and
reporting and audit requirements to assure that the asemblers conformed to the
rules of the game. These rules were spelled out in specLfLe legislation and
regulations governing the industry or in negotiated contracts between the
government and the Lndustry. This was the typical entry level approach of
countries such as Argentina, Bratil, Mexlco, Malaysia and Colombia.
3.   with some exceptions, the  utomotive industries in thes  countries have
remained domestically focused, witb minor exports of assembled vehicles or
autoparts. They have neither surmounted inegficLencies by world standards nor
produced the technology development or employment origiaally envisaged.
Autoparts producers, generally under license from foreign firms most often
produce low to moderate technology parts. With protective barriers there bas
been little incentive to modernise and upgrade thelr productlon technologically.
in short these domestically focuse industries remained largely unlinked to the
world auto industry.
UmeraLna Trepds in the World Auto Induutr¶.
4.   In the 1980's, there has been dramatic change in the auto industry, largely
brought about by the emerging dominance of the Japanese producers, who represent.
40% of the LDC market, with a pervasive presence, for examplo, in the ASNAW.
(ee Takie 1). Their presence is also felt in Latin AmerLca, where Nlsan Mas
recently announced as $1 billlion investment expansoi in Mexico and Bonda has
announced an investment in a motorcycle plant there. Moreover, in Colombia,
XMad is one of the three major assemblers. Japanese producers have brought nsw



-174-
la Asmb  ad Pa
Page 37 of 41
quality standards to the industry, now production methods an  new systems for
inventory control and distrLbutLon. The latter, the *just-in-tim* system
pioneered by Toyota, will have a signiticnut effect on LUC autopart producers
seeking to export to the industrialized countrLes. In addition, the Japonmee
producers have compressed the time required to introduce new models.
5.   The changes in the industry brought about by the Japanese producers is
forcing other producers to adopt similar approaches which at the extreme will
leave the asembler as super design" shops, with incroeaed responsibility for
design  and technological development passed  down to  a select  1lst of
tranenational autopart producers such as Bosch. This implies a hierarchlcal
order of autoparts suppliers, with most WC producers as a second tier supplier
to one of these first-tier transnationals (TNCs. )M
6.   There will be significant opportunities for autopart firms in developing
countries only to the extent that they build their *software, capablities,
through applied research and development, development of product design and
innovation and utilizatLon of modern manufacturing methods, such as total quallty
control. This implies that restructuring efforts in the autopart lndustry in
WDCs will need to focus on managerial capabilltiso.
7.   At the same time, there are real market opportunities for LDC autopart
producers in the lses rigorous aftermarket industry, particularly ln the US.
This will require, however, that these producers establish distribution,
warehousing and direct sales service capability within thelr target market. For
most LDC producers, this will require cooperative marketing efforts in order to
defray fixed overhead costs and to provide a broad range of parts to interested
customers. Taiwan, for example, exported approximately $400 million to the U.S.
after parts market in 1987.1
8.   Another trend is the increasing potential for regionalization of the auto
industry. Japanese producers within the ASMU, for example, are ncreasingly
seeking rationalixation of parts production throughout the region in what is
being termed "the brand-to-brand complementation scheme".1

Strateales of Successful DevelMolna Countries
9.   In the 1980s, with the debt crisis, the emergence of outward-looking
development philosophies in some of the NICe and the increasing sophistication
of their industrial capability, there has emerged a strong differentiation of
strategies and performance between developing countries with respect to their
auto industry. For example, due to macro-economio instability and a secular
decline in industrial growth and performance, the auto industry in Argentina has
also decline despite its early development in that country. A number of TNCs
have chosen to leave the Argentine market and its exports have decline from
approximately lS,000 units a year in 1974 to almost nothing in 1988.Y
10.  However, a number of success stories have also merged.  After suffering
through a dramatic decline in domestic demand as a result of its debt crisis in
1982, Mexico's auto industry is on the point of take off. Through a series of
regulatory decrees (1983, 1984 and 1990), the Government of Mexico has focused
the industry towards exports and has turned the balance of payments in the sector
from a deficit in excess of $2 billion in 1981 to a surplus in excess of US$1.5
billion in 1988. Exports of assembled vehicles have risn from 18,000 units in
1980 to 173,000 units in 1988, as the big three auto producers in the U.S. use
Maxico as a base from which they export to the U.S. Mexico ha benefltted from
over US$l billion of direct foreign investment (DFI) in the sector from 1986-
1988, and the projects on hand at present are in excss of US$2 billion for the
next few years. The lndustry has a world class engine capacity (exports of over
US$1 billion a year through vehicles assembled in the country), a large
aualadom or in bond segment along the U.S. border, and an autoparts industry
which is now restructurLng to keep pace. Recent legislation (1989), has further
liberalixed the sector to encourage high technology direct foreign institution
in the autoparts industry, lower domestic content requirements, and allow auto
producers to import fully assembled cars (CBUs) in line with their foreign
exchange performance. This 'guided liberalixation' has recived the endorement
of the industry in the form of the large scale investment programs underway.
11. The Korean industry has pursued quite a different strategy in an effort to
be less reliant on TECs. There are three producers in Korea - Hyundai, Daewoo
and Zia. Uyundai and Kla have minority participations from TWO producrs,
Mitsubishi and lord-Mazda, respectively. Daewoo in 50% owned by General  otors.
Bach of thes  enterpries exports.  Hyundai is the notable success story
producing 647,000 units in 1988, of which 407,715 units were exported and its car
production has been a strong streamline to the domestic autparts induutry. Since
1974, the industry has moved from 30% domestic content to over 97% at present.
Hyundai has acquired technology through licensing rather than through joint
venture. It has pursued a strategy of licensing from diversified sources, so as
not to be dependent on one major licensor.  over the period 1962-1982, it
negotiated 57 licensing agreements, with sources of technology from 9 different
countries. It is also agressively supporting research and development and new
product and design innovations.  Hyundai has pursued an independent path in

direct competition with the major producers, while Daewoo has operated under
General Motors' corporate umbrella-L
12. In a variation on this theme, the Malaysian auto industry ln the last
decade has moved from a base of 11 assemblers importing kito, to just a few
independent assemblers ln an effort to roach economic scale of production. Th
assemblers' market share has been absorbed by a natLonal champion, the Proton
saga, developed through a joLit venture wlth the state owne company RIdCO and
MitsubishL.  The Proton is a middle class car produced, at present, for the
domestic market with productLon ln 1989 of approximately 7S,000 units, or 63% of
the market. Autoparts productlon in Malaysia li beLng supported by uslng the
Ptoton as a base. Proton itself is producing a number of critical parts, as well
as body stamping..
B. Structure and Performanc of  olmbLan Vehicle Industry
13.  The vehicle iadustry conslsts ofs three assemblers -- 80FlA, Coluotores
and cc& - all of whom are prlvately held since sale ln 1989 by the Instltuto de
Fomento Industrial (IIP) of lts Government held shares in SOFASA to Renault.

limited range of vehlcles, vans, trucks and buses based on CEDe. The production
of all types of vehLcles in 1988 wa approximately 60,000 unlts, down from a high
in 1982 of approximately 75,000 units. Imports account for about 10% of sales.
The scale of productLon li well below mlnimum world standax4o. Nevertheless,
internatLonal consultants analyxing the efflciency of the assembly iadustry in
ColombLa characterLze lt as potentlally competitive verwus import of fully
assembled vehLcles. Thls is because of the freight advantages in shippLng CmDe,
The relatLvely low added value Ln assembly, and the low domestLi content of
nationally produced auto parts is low. Cnly SOfIA has integrated major vehLcle
components such as the motor and drive train.  There is negllgible export
potential percelved at present in assmbled vehLeles, though possibLilties apear
better in qpecialty vehliles such as campers, heavy trucks and buses. Due
productlon would depend on the Government and the major citLes adopting a
comprehensive transportation strategy to replace the agLng fleet of publicly
owned buses and a national tariff policy to allow them to compete wlth a number
of privately owned fleets that have emerged on an ad hoc basLs.
14.  The industry also conaLits of autoparts producers, generating approximately

aftermarket 26%, tires for assembled vehicles 2%, exports 2%. The industry is

15. in terms of overall economlc impact, the auto lndustry *mploys soe 16,000
workers in 163 dlfferent enterprLses (including those wlth 10 or more workers),

revenues, ranking second and fLrst ln their respective importance by category of
revenue. Cars are generally sold to the top 10% of the country with respect to
ncome distribution and demand is seen as relatively inelastic to price.
16. In accord with the assembly polliy framework descrLbed above the Government
has sought to develop the industry through a combination of negotiated agremente
or contracts, hlgh levels of trade protection and access to preferential credits.
The present regulatory environment includes trade protectlon, lLceninag permits,
domestLc content requirements and a positive 1Lst of domestically supplied parts
that have to be procured by local assemblers  regulatLons governing the type of
vehicles to be produed and the number of models by producrs and price controls.
17.  Recently, the G      mnt has moved to liberalLse and to Lncrease the
flexibliLty of its auto lndustry program by making agreement wlth assemblers
more uniform and flexible reducing prLoe controls (price ocontrol are in effect
for tlres used for heavy vehicles); selling the State's participation ln the
industry; reducLng regulatlon on direct forelgn lnvestment  restructurlng
tarlffs and import regulatlonst and reduction of subsidzled Latereat on loans to
the industry.

C. Indicative Reutructurlno and Develogment gtrateaw

Introduction
1.   Colombia's banking system includes 26 comnercial banks, 9 savings and loan
associations (Corporaciones de Ahorro y vivienda-CAVIs) and a central mortgage
bank (Banco Central Hipotecarlo-BCH), 24 development banks (Corporaciones
Financieras--Cls), nearly 30 trade finance companies (Companias de LinancLamiLnto
Comercial--CFCe), and the Central Bank (Banco de la Republica- BR), which plays
a signLficant role in credit markets ln addLtion to lts functlons as monetary
authority. In addition the financlal sector includes about 70 inLurance
companies, several mutual funds and three stock exchanges (at Bogota, Call and
medellin. The commercial banks have a large branch network with more than 1900
dLstrLbuted throughout Colombia. What appears to be a relatively well-developed
and dLversified financlal sector, presents some structural defLeLencies and hlgh
degrees of concentratlon that have prevented a more actlve particlpatlon in the
financing of the productive sectors. CommercLal banks represent the dominant
segment, accounting for nearly two-thLrds of the financial sector assets and 60%
of total credit, excluding houesng mortgages. Among them the public banks account
for 58% of banking assets and five of those public banks, ineluding Banco de
Colombia which was nationalized after the crisis of 1982-1983, account for 53%
of total banking assets. Foreign participation is relatively minor in the banking
sector and the 8 bans with foreign participation account for only 8% of banking
assets. Among the private banks the four largest (Bogota Oe¢idente, Bancoquia and
Industrial Colomblano), account for 25% of total banking assets and for 62% of
all the assets in the 16 private and foreign banks.
2.   All financlal institutions are subject to the regulatory framework of the
Superintendency of Banks (Superintendencia Bancaria). The Superintendency is
responsible, inter alia, for monitoring the procedures, practices, solvency and
liquidity of all such institutLons. It exercisee this responsibility mainly vLat
(a) its authorlty to approve the creation , restructuring and liquidation of
flnancial institutions; (b) setting regulations and technical norms to meet
standards of operation and accountability, and (c) its authority to suspend
operations of an institution found to be ln violation of its statutes, financial
or operational requirements. Facilities are available through the BR to maintain
liquidity requirements and, where an Lnstitution's financial and operating
condition is endangered, the Superintendency has wide latitude to intervene to
protect the interests of creditors and maintain its viability.
3.   In recent years, i'nd partially as a result of Bank projects, the
superintendency has undergoBie a process of strengthening and of reinforcement of
its procedures. Three major steps ln such reinforcement ares Decree 2041, from
1987, which regulates the,existence and operation of Corporaciones Financieras;
Resolution 3600, form 1988, which established a unified accounting standard for
all institutLons in the financial sector, and Resolution 2053, from early 1989,
which established procedures and criteria for evaluation and classification of
portfolio. The enforcement of these procedures, added to the strengthning of the
SB, both in personnel and equipment, is beginning to give a real meaning to its
regulatory role and has certalnly been instrumental in the improvement



-180-
Pag 2 of 5
experienced by the banknag system in 1967-1989. The recant regulations provide
for a close monitoring ina debt/equity ceillngs, minimum equLty requiremente,
provisions in non-performing assets according to qualLty of portfolio, limits on
distribution of profits, auditing procedures, etc. It also places limitations on
loans to related parties and the amounts of total loans outstanding to any sin&,4e
borrower. The use of a unique set of accounting standards would, certainly,
facilitate monitoring not only of individual instLtutions but of the ilnancial
sectox as a whole.
4.   For the proposed project all participating financial intermediaries (Pus)
would be required to be in good standing with the Superintendency of Banks. in
addition they would be reguired to ensure satisfactory organization and staffing
-for loan appraisal and supervision. After satisfying the eligibility criteria,
the PFis would be allowed substantial free-_liits of subproject approval. The
emphasis c' the second-tier mechantsm controls would concentrate in the overall
procedures, in the ex-post impact of the subloans and in the solvency of PFI.
The following sections highlight any additional eligibility requirements for
their participation..
Private Develoment Bank8 (CFs).
5.   Colombia has 24 develcpment banks (Corporaciones Financieras), which were
established between 1959 and 1975, to provide medium and long term credit and
equity financing to private productive enterprises, principally in the industrial
sector. Out of these 24 CMs, 6 are public ', and account for 50% of total
assets. Of the remaining 18, nine -have been beneficaries of Bank Group
financing. They I account for 78% of the private C's assets and for 39% of the
systems' assets. Bank DFC lending to Colombla, vi- Banco de la Republica, began
in 1966 and, to date, nine loans totalling US$582.5 million have been
intermediated by nine CFs. Th -;have bad a good record of performance in terms
of loan appraisals and supervision capabilities.
6.   Recent Financial Performance.   These nine CFs have in recent years
performed reasonably well. After consistent efforts, siAce 198S, toward financial
recovery in the wake of the international recession of the early 1980s, they have'
posted two successive years of healthy growth, in 1987 -1988.-They have sound
overall capital structures, with an average debt-to-equity ratio, in December
1988, of 7.4*1 (see table 2.33). They are all in cfxmpliance with eligibility
criteria set up by the Bank for Loan 2477-00 (Ninti DFC Loan) which are more
stringent than those of the SBs minimum equity of ColS500 -million, debt-to-
equity ratio under 10 l, and a specialization ratio  long anQ medium term credit
over short-term credit) over 55% ---these criterial were subsequently adopted
under Decree 2041 of 1987.  Their profIts in 1989 amounted to 2.7% of total
assets, down slightly from 2.5, in 1988, and their administrative expenses have
been low, particularly in comparison with the rest of Colombia's financial
system, averaging 1.38% of total assets over the period 1985-1989. The average
indebtnesse for the group is 7.4 times equity.
'They include: Cofiagro, IFI, Popplar, Transporte, Turismo and Electrica.
$ Valle, suramerLcana, Progz.so, Caldas, Colombiansa, Santander, Oc¢idente,
Nacional y Norte.

8.    CF Reform Program. While MC were intended as primarily long-term lenders
and principal usera of BR rediscounts, the inflationary environment and high
liquidity preferences of savers has made mobilisation of medium and lont term
domestic resources extremely difficult. In 1975, CFs were given autbority to
issue short term deposits (CDTs) to finance the working capital needs of
productive enterprises. In practice, this provided an incentive to turn toward
the short term end of the market. In the early 1980. short-tem credit by CFs
(with maturities of up to 3 years according to the SD definition existing before
1987) represented nearly 70% of total credit, After 1987, the 8B changed the
definition of short-term to include only maturities of up to one year, but also
established an specialization requisite of at least SO% of loans with maturities
in excess of one year. Today, maturities in excess of one year account for nearly
80%  of CFs' credit. For participating CFO, uslig  Banks  funds, a  5S
specialization ratio was already established in 1985, as one of the eligibility
criteria.
9.   In October 1987, through Decre  2041/87, the Government adopted a Reform
Program for the CT system. The program was consistent with earlier
recoumendatiomns by the 3ank, aimed at ,reasserting the original role of Cds as
longer-term financiers and equity Investors. It was designed to increase their
solvency, through forced capitalization and through more stringent accounting of
liabilities, and their profitability via the diversiflcation of it. markets and
financial services. The Reform authorizes CPU to finance leasing companies, to
develope such services as factoring, underwriting, and enterprise restructuring-
advisory services.
10.  The 1987 Reform established the following three standards whicb all CP1
must meet, according to a graduz-l schedule, between 1988-90s
(a)   specialization ratio - at leapt 50 percent of a CF's operations
involving its own and BR rediscount funds must carry maturities in
excess of one yearu

(b)   mini.mum capltaliLatlon of existing Cps of  ol$ 700 million (US$ 1.9
million) by December 1989, and Col05,000 mllilon (US$2.6 mllion) ln
December 1990. Col$ 1,000 mllion for any new CT establlshoed after
October 1987, and
(c)   a maximum debt to equlty ratio of 101 Lnstead of the prevloue 5s:1.
Nearly all of the prlvate C01 now meet these standards and are expected to be
eligible as PFIs for the Project.
11. In addition to financial crLteria, and in vLew of the speclallzed type of
financing to be provided uw&der the restructurlng projec&t, eligible PFis would
have to demonstrate a satLsfactory organLzation and staffing, a capablilty to
prepare, or help prepare, *workout strategies", and a capability to handle
appraisal and supervision procedures. CFs dieplay generally good experlence in
appraLsal and surpervision work and have some participation  on workouts to
assist  ome of their problem. clients.  Their experlence with a systematic
approach to workouts, financial engineerLng and investment banking is rather
limLted but practical. Their staffing is of good professLonal callber combinlng
solid trainLng with ample exposure to business practlcae. Wlth the help of some
initial and specialized training and the assistance of project-speclflc
consultants, all of the CF0 are antLiLpated to be able to provide good cuality
development banking services for the Project.
Commercial Banks.
12.   The 26 commerclal banks ln Colombia accounted for nearly two-thirds of the
financial sector assets. Public banks, including anceo 4d Colombia account for
S8% of banking assets. Their large network qo branch offices facilitates their
access to low cost demand, saving and time deposLts. As these represent about
70% of thelr sources of funds, about two-thirds of their loans are also short-
term (maturities of less than one year). These institutions have suffered from
a variety of distortions in the financial sector - ln partlcular, the forced
lnvestment and directed credit system, dlfferentlal interest rate po)4cies
whlch have negatively influenced efficiency, profitability and capital stiucture.
13.   These underlying problems were clearly exposed by the recession of the
early 19805, when banks had to confronts (a) the major repayment problems
incurred by their borrowers, which were hit by the combination of high real
interest rates and stagnating demand for their products    and (b) a high
portfolio concentration in a limlted number of firm  and the covoequences of
relaxed credit standards which had been applied to related clients.   The
resulting financial crisls led to a prolonged deterioration ln the system, which
did not show signs of reversal until 1985, and forced Government interventLon in
several major banks (Colombia, Istado, Comercio, Teguendama and Trabajadores) in
an effort to protect depositors and the functioning of the banklng system.
14.   The profitability as a share of paid capital and legal reserves fell
steadlly from 29.0W in 1981 to negative levels in 198c& (-188.1%) and 1986
(-7.4%). Non-performing assets in relation to paid capital and legal reserves
rose from 35.3% to 381.2%. Net capltal (paid capital plus legal reserves loes
non performing assets net of provisions/paLd capital and reserves) fell over this
period from 82.S to -35.2 percent. By the end of 1986, loans in arrears exceedint

one year represented 16.2% of the system's portfolio. The overall figures,
however, are severely affected by Banco de Colombia, the largest private bank in
Colombia before its nationalization in 1984. Excluding anco de Colombia, profits
in private banks aad in foreign banks, went also through a low in 1984-1985 but
were never negative.
15.   Since 198S, the banking system started a sustalned recovery. Net capital
and total arrears as a share of total portfolio have returned to their 1982 level
56.8 percent and 6.3 percent, respectLvely, by the end of 1988. Debt-to-equity
ratios varies substantially between the dlfferent types of banks (3.5X1 for the
nationalized banks to nearly 16s1 for two offLcial banks--Popular and Ganadero).
Recent dLsposLtions from the Superintendency (Resol. 21/1989) have reduced the
mandatory ratio from 15:1 to 12.5:1, effective July 1989. Twenty two out of 26
banks regiLtered profits in 1988 and in the first half of 1989. Profltability in
prlvate local and forelgn banks is now at very satisfactory levels-having reached
67% and 34.5%, respectlvely, in June 1989. Nevertheless, in spite of a
significant reduction (from 8.5% of assets in 1987 to 4.9% in 1988), arrears are
still important. It Is expected that the new regulations on portfolio evaluation
by the SB will induce further and more permanent improve  nts in the portfolio
qualLty of comercial banks. Total adeinistrAtive expenses are still very high at
4.8% of total assets for the total banking system. They show little variation
between the different groups of banks. In an oligopolistic market, inefficiencies
are passed-over to the clients tJtrough the charging of very high spreads (7-9%,
in real term). The large reserve requirements and forced investments, have
obscured the issue of efficiency.
16. Recent Government measures to increase capitalization of banks, as well as
announcement of reprivatisation of natLonalixed banks and of measures to
facilitate foreign investment I- the financial sector, are expected to increas.O
compaetition and efflciency. Under the increasing strength of the Superintendency
of Banks in its regulatory functions, the growth of competition should not be at
the expense of solvency and profltabi-lity of the banking institutions.
17.  Five camuercial banks have expreasd an interest in partici*Ating as
financial intermediaries in the proposed ProjectO. Commercial banks are already
important creditors to industry, can expand subotantially the ability of CMe t
finance the volume of restructuring investments expected to take place under the
trade reform program, and wish in many cases to do so as part of their own
institutional development. The eligibilLty criteria for their participatLon
would be as stringent as in the case of CFa. PartLLpating banks would be
requlred to be in full compliance with operating standard set firth by the SB,
and to demonstrate satisfactory organization and staffing to prepare or help
prepare restructuring loans. While few banks have had substantial experience in
long-term lending, they are prepared to undergo the necesary traLning and
reorganization neoded to tackle long-term project lending. While the financLal
status of participating banks would be certifled by the Superintendency of Banks,
BR (the second-tier institutLon) would verify the technical capabilties of PFTs.

3nvxronmental Pollution Control Proarum (US$2 million administered by DNP).
1.          Colombia in experiencing severe environmental deterioration, whioh
is among others, caused by a lack of pollution control planning and enforcement.
Many of its waterways are contaminated by sewage, industrial effluent, pesticides
and oil leaks. A&ctual levels of contamination are in all likelihood far higher
than those officially reported, given that common pollutants from chemical and
petrochmical industries are not taken into account by present asssament
proceduress for instance, synthetic organics are not even monitored and there
are no regulations related to hazardous wastes.   other major pollution.
nerating lndustries aro those of the steel, textiles, pulp and paper tanneries
and food processing sectors.
2           Governmental pollution control activities are weak, and for everal
years old industrial emission standards have not been enforced. In the past,
there seems to have been an absence of political support for coping with long
standing industrial resistance and inertia in the area- of pollution control.
Nevertheless,, ome progress was achieved on the legislative side, especially with
reard to water protection, for which the legislation now in place provides sound
instruments and policies.
3.          There is an absnce of operational procedures and actions within
governmental agencies which are needed to give--real meaning to the pollution
control prmits required for new investmnts. There i also little follow up of
requisLtes and periodical renewal to assure compliance. There is no control on
hazardous waste, and air quality management is also ineffective.
Institutional Agnea
4.          Shortcoming  in management, as well as som  unresolved inter-
institutional issues, also slow down the operationalization of enforcement
measures. Several agencies operate in pollution control matters:
(a)   the central agency for environmental protection, the Instituto
Nacional de lo Recursos Naturales y del Mbdio Rmbients - INDXRMNM,
a weak institution which lacks the budget, staff, political clout,
and the continuity of leadership necessary for offectively carrying
out its numerous responsibilities; INDBREMA has only 5 pollution
oontrol engineers, out of more than 2,000 employeess

(b) the Department for Environmental sanitation, a Ministry of Health
Unit, which is supposed to control air pollution and to establish
standards to protect human healthj its activities in the pollution
control area have been very limited and seem to be decreasing; and
(c)  regional authorites.
5.         RegLonal Autonomous Corporations have been created frQm 1954 to 1983.
In Colombia, as well as Bollvia, their powers rapldly increased in the last
decade, a movement that is likely to contlnue, constituting local authorities
with state governments characteristies. The first one, Corporacion Autonomsa
Regional del Cauca -CVC- was inspired in the Tennessee Valley Authority - TVA.
step by step, as externalities began to threaten economic development and public
health, a new institutional framework to cope with environmental management has
been drafted. Regional authorities quietly took on pollution control activities,
leading to the 1989 proposal of a major sectorial restructuring law empowering
them to act under the supervision of a new federal agency. A 1989 study propoed
a compreenslve reorganization of environmental agencies and thair functions,
which was to include the creation of the Autonomous Department for Renewable
Natural Resources and Environment (DAPEAR).
6.         However, recent  elect$ons  and  consequent  changes  of  higher
governmental officers have further delayed a decdison on this proposed law, which
Ln September was in it. 5th revision. If this proposed law were to be enacted,
the whole restructuring of the sector would realistically take several years to
becom operationalI once empowered to implement environmental protection policies
under the coordination of the DARIAR, most regional authorities would need to
build up their capabiliti e, which presently do not exist in most cases.
7.   Dgg0zaM"iaLgn.   8ven without the enactment of the proposed law,
decentralizatLon seems irreversible. Some corporations are legally empowered to
act on environmental protection; others have dlfferent levels and kLids of
responsibilities through specific agreements with INDtRhN. To date, only 2 out
of 18 regional authorities have some significant work on pollution controls
Corporacion Autonoma Regional del Cauca, CVC and Corporacion Autonoma Regional
de las cuencas de los Rios Bogota, Ubate y Suares (CAR). And even these lack
personnel, training and equlpment to cope with a substantial part of pollution
control requirements, from planning to monitoring, from extent (different
sources) to quallty (different pollutants).  Both of them concentrate their
efforts on organlc loads.  Traditioral sanitary concepts and technoloies
(extended aeration, activated sludge) have been adopted, even though such
substances/compounds are not removed through, pretreatment systems. Therefore,
standards for several among the more dangerous pollutants included An local
regulatLons have never been enforced for lack of training and laboratories.

IndustrLal Restructuring *od Developaent Project
Public Siect;or SUuot= For, Retkucturina
Proiect _onLtrXin9 Protram (US$1 million adminiatered by MED)
1.          The trade reform and industrial restructuring programs formulated
over the past two years reflect the most explicit, concerted effort by Colombia
to define and redirect its industrial policy since the adoption of the import
substitution strategy of the 19504 The MLnistry of Economic Development (ZGD)
has played an important role in this process and must continue to do so through
daily administration and coordination to oversee their implementation. Although
the MED was recently reorganized to undertake this function, its human and
budgetary resources have changed little. Under the Public sector support for.
Restructuring Component of the Project, provisions would be made to implement a
Project Monitoring Program (P8P) within MED to (a) regularli monitor the
performance of enterprises receiving Project resources and of- industry overall
to the trade reform and modernization process, (b) maintain regular conoultations
-With producer associations and provide advisory services to enterprises primarily
to identify fine tuning measures to reduce bottlenecks to the supply rOsponse,
and (c) maintain coordination with other Governmental entities to encourage
further changes in policies as needed to  achieve internationrW competitiveness.
2.          The Performance Analysis Division of the PMP would establish a
monitoring system and regular analyses of basic performance trends and reactions
to policy adjustments of the industrial sector and major aubsectors. The data
collected by existing statistical services (eq., DANM1   INCOMEX),  covering
employment and wages, value added, raw materials utilized,->4ports and exports,
credit approved, etc., as well as other leading Indicators wlll be the main
sources of information for this purpose. To monitor the restructuring investment
process, a special survey of enterprises and financial institutions will be
developed to track the character of such investments and the enterprises
undertaking them, the evolution of technology employed, t'he resulting
productivity and competitiveness changes, and their overall performance. The
reporting system will also include periodically international indicators of
productivity and international competitiveness of major industrial product lines
in comparator countries purchased from appropriate scurces.
3.          A significant share of the proposed Project was developed through the
collaboration of the public and private sectors in Subsector Working, Groups.
They were particularly helpful in overseeing work to identify the influence of
public policies on enterprise competitiveness and in defining -the broad
requirements for restructuring. Both the MED and industry wish to continue the
close consultation developed under this structure.   Thus, the process of
preparing subsector studies used for Project preparation will be replioated for
other subsectors during Project implementation and used as a forum to discuss
restructuring issues of concern to both sides.  To Support this process, the
Restructuring Division of the PMP would -provide advisory services under this
frameiork to industries aimed to identify major bottlenecks and propose solutions

A. Industrial Restructurina Policy Framework
1.    Processing time for applications for Plan Vallejo (Ministry of

contracts (NED).
2.    Industrial production coverage of QRs (NED).
3.    Number of rates, average and maximum tariffs (including Law 75
surcharge) by industrial subsector (Ministry of Finance).
4.    Integration requirement (percentage) of locally supplied parts in
passenger and commercial vehicles assembled in Colombia (NED).
B. Credit Prooram (BR)
1.    Number and name of Project participating financial intermediaries
(PFI), and volume of enterprise loan/equity rediscounts by BR for each.
2.    Number and characteristics of each investment financed by each PFI -
new or existing enterprise, business activity, asset and employee size,
location, local or export market orientation.
3.    Financing plan of investment - share of resources from enterprise,
PIi, PIP, FCE and World Bank;
4.   Use of World Bank subloan proceeds - fixed investment, working
capital, construction, intangibles and technical assistance.
5.    Subloan characteristics - interest rates, grace and maturity.
6.    Average subloan processing time (from PVT to BR rediscount and to
World Bank as required).
s Agency to prepare analysis for each indicator is noted in parenthesis.

7. As part of the Project Completion Report required within 6 months after
final disbursement, an evaluation by BR in c6llaboration with PFrs of the
impact of enterprise investments via a sample of not less than 10% of
total subloan and equity participations financed under the Project. This
analysis would address inter alia (a) sales and profitability trends since
subloan/equity approval (including the recalculation of the investment's
FRR), (b) changes in enterprise competitiveness (reduced product costs,
improved quality and service) and the restructuring measures to achieve
them, (c) employment creation and wage rate trends, and (d) changes in
domestic/export market orientation.
C. Public Sector Supnort for Restructurino
1. Labor Adjustment Assistance Program - (a) volume, location and profile
of groups receiving assistance (individuals dismissed, enterprises re-
employing dismissed individuals, community development); (b) number and
type of organizations administering assistance; (c) number, type and cost
of services provided (skill assessment and job counselling, job search and
referral, entrepreneurship programs, job retraining); and (d) time period
unemployed of individuals dismissed and profile of new job found (SENA).
2. Environmental Pollution Control Program - number, area, and summary of
conclusions of screening of environmental aspects of industrial
investments; actions taken and compliance by private enterprises to reduce
industrial pollution; and level and nature of training of Regional
Corporation staff (DNP);
3. Project Monitoring Program - prepare reports detailing the number,
position, activities and outputs of staff to (a) monitor the performance
of  the  industrial   sector  toward  increasing  its   international
competitiveness; (b) assist producers, inter alia through subsectors
working Groups, to achieve smooth implementation of. the rRDP; and (c) to
identify bottlenecks occurring in the preparation and implementation of
enterprise investments, and recommendations for public policy and
institutional fine tuning (NED).
